</p><p>
 Supported by
</p><p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <a class="css-1riqqik e1jsehar0" href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/mekado-murphy">
  <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
   Mekado Murphy
  </span>
 </a>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 AUSTIN, Tex. — Surprises abounded at this year’s South by Southwest film festival, with movies featuring depraved demons, devilish doppelgängers and sadistic senseis all doing despicable things. And yet, one of the most gasp-worthy moments was hearing the tween actor Jacob Tremblay use the F-word in the first five minutes of “Good Boys.” It wouldn’t be the only time in this comedy about a wild day in the life of a few sixth-graders, and it was one of several breakout titles worth knowing about as the film portion of the festival comes to a close on Saturday.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “If I’m being honest, I am shocked and appalled,” Tremblay joked during the post-screening Q.&amp; A., and added referring to his director Gene Stupnitsky, “Gene told me this was going to be a PG-rated family movie. He lied.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The movie was right at home at a festival that has turned into something of a testing ground for the hit potential of Hollywood comedies. The audience applauded the comic timing of Tremblay and his co-stars, Brady Noon and the scene-stealing Keith L. Williams, and that positive reaction along with a recently released
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rF1F9dIbNqM" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  red-band trailer
 </a>
 featuring one of the movie’s producers, Seth Rogen, has fueled social-media interest. “Good Boys” opens nationwide in August.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Another talker was Olivia Wilde’s directing debut, “Booksmart.” Much like “Good Boys,” it follows students on a wild day, but this one is female-focused. Beanie Feldstein and Kaitlyn Dever star as high school seniors who, on the last day of school, realize they may have studied too hard and partied too softly. They make up for that in one outrageous evening that includes drugged strawberries and a raucous graduation bash. The packed house at the Paramount theater especially appreciated the killer soundtrack and a high laugh-per-minute ratio.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Also winning over festivalgoers was “Long Shot,” a rom-com starring Rogen
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/03/12/movies/charlize-theron-long-shot-sxsw.html?module=inline" title="">
  and Charlize Theron
 </a>
 . The crowd was drawn to the combination of smart and slapstick humor, and was also roused by a Boyz II Men cameo. Then, after the screening, could it really happen? Or do dreams just fade away? The pop group did a little East Coast swing in a surprise performance.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The laughs had a mellower edge at the premiere of the Harmony Korine comedy “The Beach Bum,” starring Matthew McConaughey as Moondog, the pothead writer he may have been born to play. The crowd basked in the movie’s hazy glow, and McConaughey was a great sport, attending the after-party dressed in character. Critical reaction to the film was a little more mixed.
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://variety.com/2019/film/reviews/the-beach-bum-review-matthew-mcconaughey-1203158560/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Variety wrote,
 </a>
 “The question here isn’t if McConaughey is having fun, but whether audiences will find his laid-back, ever-lit persona nearly as entertaining to watch.” And
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.hollywoodreporter.com/review/beach-bum-review-1193429" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  The Hollywood Reporter said
 </a>
 that the film was “high on style, if not substance.”
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Humor was also a thread through some of the festival’s darker films, including
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/03/09/movies/jordan-peele-us-premiere.html?module=inline" title="">
  Jordan Peele’s “Us,”
 </a>
 which opened SXSW and featured a measured number of laugh- and shriek-worthy sequences. Similarly, audiences winced and laughed during “The Art of Self-Defense,” starring Jesse Eisenberg in a deadpan role as an accountant who is brutally attacked by masked assailants. Taking classes at a dojo, he meets a sensei (Alessandro Nivola) with a tainted worldview. The comedy is pitch-black, but the crowd appreciated its sinister quirks.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Other funny journeys down twisted roads included “Porno,” a 1992-set film about conservative Christian teenagers working in a small-town movie theater. After discovering an old film hidden in the walls, they inadvertently unleash a sex demon that wreaks havoc. Keola Racela’s debut feature, with more than one instance of genital mutilation played for laughs, made festivalgoers squirm but got them talking as well.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “Villains,” directed by Dan Berk and Robert Olsen, put a fun twist on the home-invasion genre, with Bill Skarsgard and Maika Monroe as criminals on the lam. When their car runs out of gas, they pick the wrong home to break into, that of a couple (Jeffrey Donovan and Kyra Sedgwick) with a few skeletons in their closet and another secret in their basement. And in keeping with the Boys/Boyz festival motif, there was “Boyz in the Wood,” a hallucinogenic comedy-survival tale from the music video director Ninian Doff about teenagers in the Scottish Highlands who become more hunted than hunters. The site Film School Rejects
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://filmschoolrejects.com/boyz-in-the-wood-review/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  described it as “‘Hot Fuzz’ meets ‘Attack the Block.’”
 </a>
 The movie was the subject of so much chatter that SXSW added an extra screening.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The festival wasn’t entirely lighthearted. Its two major jury awards went to much more serious-minded films. “Alice,” Josephine Mackerras’s drama about a woman (Emilie Piponnier) who becomes a prostitute to support her child, won the narrative feature prize. And “For Sama,” which focuses on a young female Syrian filmmaker, Waad al-Kateab, who cataloged harrowing aspects of her life in Aleppo for five years, took the documentary feature award.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 But the festival’s biggest and funniest standouts show its programming strengths, and reinforce the value of the theatrical experience, the opportunity to laugh, and sometimes cringe, with a large, responsive audience.
</p><p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>
<p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>

 <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">

</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Wednesday’s
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/04/10/science/black-hole-picture.html?module=inline" title="">
  unveiling
 </a>
 of the first image of a black hole — found in the Messier 87 galaxy, roughly 55 million light-years away — has captured the imagination of scientists and amateur astronomers around the world. But of all the intriguing and humbling phenomena to be found in outer space, why are black holes such a particular fascination?
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Maybe it has something to do with science-fiction, which has long exploited the narrative possibilities offered by these
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/04/10/science/what-is-black-hole.html?module=inline" title="">
  inescapable regions of space
 </a>
 (as in the movie “High Life,” which debuts this week). What follows is a look at some of the best streamable movies in the black hole canon: We can’t guarantee all the science, but we can tell you where to find them.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 [
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/04/10/science/black-hole-picture.html?module=inline" title="">
  <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
   Read about the first ever image of a black hole.
  </em>
 </a>
 ]
</p>

  How to watch:
 </strong>

  iTunes
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.vudu.com/content/movies/#!content/13829/The-Black-Hole" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Vudu
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://play.google.com/store/movies/details?id=0WjAWDVaYcA" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Google Play
 </a>
 and
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch/?v=0WjAWDVaYcA" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  YouTube
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Walt Disney Productions had
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/1979/07/22/archives/the-studio-that-mickey-built-boards-the-scifi-bandwagon-disneys.html?module=inline" title="">
  a humble goal
 </a>
 for this (then)
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/1979/03/04/archives/disney-studio-risks-175-million-on-black-hole-exhibitors-shown-ad.html?module=inline" title="">
  big-budget production
 </a>
 : Two years after “Star Wars,” the company wanted a blockbuster space opera of its own. According to the book “The Mouse Machine,” by J. P. Telotte, the studio took notice of the increasing chatter in scientific circles about black holes (Archibald Wheeler had
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2008/04/14/science/14wheeler.html?module=inline" title="">
  coined the term
 </a>
 in 1967), revising the original script and focusing on the crew of a deep space mission and its discovery of not only a black hole but also another ship that somehow managed to elude the gravitational pull. Reviews were
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/1979/12/21/archives/screen-black-hole-by-disneyultimate-knowledge.html?module=inline" title="">
  mixed
 </a>
 , and the film
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.boxofficemojo.com/movies/?id=blackhole.htm" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  recouped only its costs
 </a>
 . But it did introduce an important new element to Disney filmmaking:
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/1979/12/16/archives/the-black-hole-casts-the-computer-as-moviemaker.html?module=inline" title="">
  computer-generated animation
 </a>
 .
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  How to watch:
 </strong>
 Stream it on
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.criterionchannel.com/videos/a-brief-history-of-time" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  The Criterion Channel
 </a>
 ; rent or buy it on
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://a.co/d/98JRbDM" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Amazon
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://gowatchit.com/movies/234577/watch_now?campaign=default-website&amp;category=rent_own_online&amp;gwi_asset=true&amp;modality=api&amp;partner_id=310&amp;provider_format_id=3&amp;provider_id=3" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  iTunes
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://play.google.com/store/movies/details?id=8Nj_S-v8Rz0" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Google Play
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.vudu.com/content/movies/details/A-Brief-History-of-Time/73989" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Vudu
 </a>
 and
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch/?v=8Nj_S-v8Rz0" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  YouTube
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Perhaps no single scientist shaped more of our contemporary thought and fascination with black holes than the theoretical physicist
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/03/14/science/stephen-hawking-timeline.html?module=inline" title="">
  Stephen Hawking
 </a>
 , whose ideas about black holes, quantum mechanics, radiation and relativity were neatly packaged in his 1988 best-seller “
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/1988/04/03/books/waht-place-for-a-creator.html?module=inline" title="">
  A Brief History of Time: From the Big Bang to Black Holes
 </a>
 .” Four years later, the director
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/1990/06/01/movies/at-the-movies.html?module=inline" title="">
  Errol Morris
 </a>
 (“The Thin Blue Line”) released his
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/1992/08/21/movies/review-film-if-you-fumbled-the-book-you-ll-love-the-movie.html?module=inline" title="">
  documentary film adaptation
 </a>
 of the book, featuring extensive interviews with Hawking and his colleagues, friends and family, intermingling his biography with vivid and ingenious visualizations of his thoughts and theories. Hawking’s work is dense and complex, but Morris’s dazzling film furthers the source book’s mission of making the science of space a bit more understandable to the layperson.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  How to watch:
 </strong>
 Stream it on
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.showtime.com/#play/3467375" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Showtime
 </a>
 ; rent or buy it on
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://a.co/d/e5WFDkO" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Amazon
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://itunes.apple.com/us/movie/event-horizon/id306163996" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  iTunes
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://play.google.com/store/movies/details?id=Jefhys1LO-U" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Google Play
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.vudu.com/content/movies/details/Event-Horizon/13543" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Vudu
 </a>
 and
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Jefhys1LO-U" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  YouTube
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The new image from Messier 87 was possible, in a large part, because of a network of radio antennas known as the Event Horizon Telescope. That name has a logical tie to its target: The event horizon is “the edge of a black hole, the point of no return,” according to the Times reporter
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/04/10/science/black-hole-picture.html?module=inline" title="">
  Dennis Overbye
 </a>
 , who notes, “beyond the event horizon, not even light can escape the black hole’s gravitational pull.” But it will summon a different connotation to movie fans. Paul W.S. Anderson’s “Event Horizon” makes black holes into
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/1997/08/15/movies/mind-bending-fiend-puts-the-warp-in-warp-speed.html?module=inline" title="">
  horror fodder
 </a>
 , telling the story of a vessel that creates an artificial black hole in order to make a space travel wormhole. Suffice to say, things go very wrong.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  How to watch:
 </strong>

 </a>
 ,

  iTunes
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://play.google.com/store/movies/details/Star_Trek?id=FTzIaSQwxCU&amp;feature=movies_movers_shakers" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Google Play
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.vudu.com/linkShare?siteID=lKqk0WGRBfI-fEuyk1BpaDYkqoFvR2cfDw&amp;url=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.vudu.com%2Fmovies%2F%23%21content%2F172148%2FStar-Trek" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Vudu
 </a>
 and
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=FTzIaSQwxCU" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  YouTube
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Science-fiction television shows, like the later iterations of “Star Trek” and “Battlestar Galactica,” have made black holes a mainstay of their storytelling. But the “Star Trek” film series hasn’t had much use for them, with the notable exception of
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2009/04/26/movies/26itzk.html?action=click&amp;contentCollection=Movies&amp;module=inline&amp;region=Marginalia&amp;pgtype=article" title="">
  J.J. Abrams
 </a>
 ’s 2009
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2009/05/08/movies/08trek.html?module=inline" title="">
  reboot
 </a>
 of the franchise. Nero (Eric Bana), the Romulan villain of the film, uses the principles of the black hole for dastardly ends, creating an artificial black hole out of “red matter” to destroy the planet Vulcan. But then a real black hole creates a time warp that sends Mr. Spock back in time to help Captain Kirk and his own younger self try to stop Nero. (It all makes sense in the movie.)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  How to watch:
 </strong>
 Rent or buy it on
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://a.co/d/4AENyHR" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Amazon
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://itunes.apple.com/us/movie/interstellar/id960891136?ign-mpt=uo%3D5" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  iTunes
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://play.google.com/store/movies/details?id=Df7IEKqimOY&amp;PAffiliateId=110l4uj&amp;PCamRefID=~310~M~267032~~default-website~" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Google Play
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.vudu.com/content/movies/details/Interstellar/608646" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Vudu
 </a>
 and
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch/?v=Df7IEKqimOY" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  YouTube
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 This Kubrick-inspired
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2014/11/05/movies/interstellar-christopher-nolans-search-for-a-new-planet.html?module=inline" title="">
  sci-fi adventure
 </a>
 from
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2014/11/02/magazine/the-exacting-expansive-mind-of-christopher-nolan.html?module=inline" title="">
  Christopher Nolan
 </a>
 hangs a key plot point on black holes: The deep-space crew at its center, led by the former NASA pilot Joseph Cooper (Matthew McConaughey), is sent to explore a wormhole leading to potentially habitable planets. But the wormhole’s proximity to a black hole causes gravitational time dilation; in the brief time Cooper and another crew member (Anne Hathaway) spend exploring one of the planets, 23 years of earth time elapse, causing duress for the scientists. Nolan, known for his meticulousness, took
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.wired.com/2014/10/astrophysics-interstellar-black-hole/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  great pains
 </a>
 to try to ensure that the science of the film checked out, and its rendering of the black hole was
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.space.com/27701-interstellar-movie-science-black-holes.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  considered
 </a>
 by many to be the most realistic yet in a fiction film.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  How to watch:
 </strong>
 Stream it on
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.netflix.com/title/80000644" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Netflix
 </a>
 ; rent or buy it on
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://a.co/d/bN4rzB2" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Amazon
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://itunes.apple.com/us/movie/the-theory-of-everything/id942799869?ign-mpt=uo%3D5" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  iTunes
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://play.google.com/store/movies/details?id=VcKLrLwE8-A&amp;PAffiliateId=110l4uj&amp;PCamRefID=~310~M~269343~~default-website~" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Google Play
 </a>
 ,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.vudu.com/content/movies/details/The-Theory-of-Everything/608656" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Vudu
 </a>
 and
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch/?v=VcKLrLwE8-A" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  YouTube
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “Mr. Morris conveys something of what Dr. Hawking thinks about black hole evaporation, imaginary time and curved space,” The Times’s Timothy Ferris
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/1992/08/16/movies/film-when-science-is-the-star.html?module=inline" title="">
  wrote
 </a>
 about Morris’s “A Brief History of Time.” “But for most viewers, these exotic ideas will be overshadowed by the human drama of the great physicist’s triumph over adversity.” The director
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/video/movies/100000003189019/directing-the-theory-of-everything.html?module=inline" title="">
  James Marsh
 </a>
 focused firmly on that drama in this
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2014/11/07/movies/in-the-theory-of-everything-stephen-hawkings-home-life.html?module=inline" title="">
  biopic
 </a>
 , with an Oscar-winning Eddie Redmayne as a young Hawking and an Oscar-nominated Felicity Jones as his future wife, Jane. As they struggle to come to terms with a motor neuron disorder that confined him to a wheelchair for the rest of his life, he develops his initial theories about the visibility of black holes — though there is not much time to get into scientific specifics.
</p><p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>
<p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>
<p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>

</p><p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
  Sara Aridi
 </span>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  The Criterion Collection unveils its new streaming service. And a documentary on a former drug dealer debuts on Starz.
 </em>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  COLUMBIA NOIR
 </strong>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  Stream on
 </em>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.criterionchannel.com/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
   Criterion Channel
  </em>
 </a>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  .
 </em>
 Fans of art-house films and classic movies lamented
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/10/29/movies/filmstruck-closing-best-movies.html?module=inline" title="">
  the end of Filmstruck
 </a>
 in November. Now they can turn to this new streaming service, featuring more than 1,000 movies from the libraries of the Criterion Collection and Janus Films and a rotating selection from major and independent studios. It starts with a spotlight on 11 noir films from Columbia Pictures, including “Nightfall” by Jacques Tourneur, “The Big Heat” by Fritz Lang and “Pushover” by Richard Quine.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  QUEENS OF MYSTERY
 </strong>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  Stream on
 </em>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://acorn.tv/queensofmystery_cs" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
   Acorn TV
  </a>
 </em>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  .
 </em>
 This
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aiQjB7xUDFg" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  whimsical mini-series
 </a>
 stars Olivia Vinall as Matilda Stone, a detective sergeant whose life has always been shrouded in mystery. When Matilda was 3, her mother disappeared, leaving her in the hands of her three crime-writing aunts, Beth (Sarah Woodward), Cat (Julie Graham) and Jane (Siobhan Redmond). She left her quiet hometown at 18 to become a cadet, and now she has returned for a new job. Her aunts are overjoyed to have her back — mostly because they want in on the action. While Matilda tries but fails to keep them out of her investigations, she uses her sleuthing skills to uncover the truth about her mother.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  WHITE BOY
 </strong>
 (2019)
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  9 p.m. on Starz.
 </em>
 In 1987, Richard Wershe Jr., a 17-year-old Detroit drug dealer known as
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.newyorker.com/news/news-desk/white-boy-ricks-parole-hearing-and-drug-war-lessons-unlearned" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  White Boy Rick,
 </a>
 was arrested. He was eventually convicted and handed a life sentence in prison. Many have questioned the severity of his punishment, particularly because Wershe had been drawn into the criminal world as a police informant at 14 and had helped bring down a local criminal organization. (He’s currently in a Florida prison on racketeering charges and is
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.politico.com/states/florida/story/2019/03/13/white-boy-rick-to-remain-in-florida-prison-for-now-910414" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  scheduled to be released
 </a>
 in 2020.) Reporters have followed his story over the years, and
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/09/11/movies/review-white-boy-rick.html?module=inline" title="">
  a drama
 </a>
 was even in theaters last fall. The tale receives fresh scrutiny in this documentary, in which journalists, federal agents and others weigh in on Wershe’s sentence and question the role of the police.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  AMERICAN DAD!
 </strong>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  10 p.m. on TBS.
 </em>
 In this Season 13 finale, Stan (voiced by Seth MacFarlane) and Francine (Wendy Schaal) try to save their marriage but wind up trapped on a hot-air balloon. The rest of the family gets caught up in a jingle-writing contest.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  THOSE WHO CAN’T
 </strong>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  10 p.m. on truTV.
 </em>
 This scripted comedy series about a group of very unprofessional high school teachers wraps up its third season. The crew devises a plan to keep the school from shutting down while Quinn (Rory Scovel) and Tammy (Sonya Eddy) set out on a vision quest. Those who haven’t seen the show should know that it isn’t “for people who like their humor refined, subtle or family-friendly,” Neil Genzlinger wrote in his
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2016/02/11/arts/television/review-on-those-who-cant-stalking-the-halls-of-lower-education.html?module=inline" title="">
  New York Times review
 </a>
 . “It goes in big on gross-out humor, crass puns and general raunchiness.”
</p><p>
 Advertisement
</p><p>
 Advertisement
</p><p>
 Supported by
</p><p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <a class="css-1riqqik e1jsehar0" href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/manohla-dargis">
  <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
   Manohla Dargis
  </span>
 </a>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Soon after I first met Agnès Varda, I drove her to the Hollywood police station. It was 2009 and we were supposed to be at a dinner for the Los Angeles opening of her documentary “The Beaches of Agnès,” a lyrical ramble through her life. At that point, Varda had been making movies for more than a half century, a milestone that then seemed to me as impossible as her death last month at 90 seems to me now.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Varda showed up late for the dinner, hurrying into the restaurant in a state of agitation. While at a Starbucks, her bag had been stolen — wallet, passport,
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  tout
 </em>
 . Her greatest concern was the digital video camera that had the images she’d shot earlier that day of her friend Sharon Stone. Dazed that Stone and Varda occupied the same corner of the universe, I got it together and took her to the police station, where she filled out a report and murmured doubts about the young desk officer.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 We returned to the restaurant for our dinner, the first of many encounters that we had over the next decade, sometimes for a leisurely meal or at a festival where invariably she was being honored. The last time I saw her was in May at her house on the Left Bank in Paris, an area that became part of her identity. Varda was routinely called the mother or grandmother or godmother of the French New Wave, and often grouped with its Left Bank flank, alongside her friends Chris Marker and Alain Resnais. She wasn’t always comfortable with the maternal moniker though she later embraced it — and why not? She had shown the way.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  [A.O. Scott on how Varda
 </em>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://www.nytimes.com/2019/03/29/movies/agns-varda-appreciation.html?module=inline" title="">
  <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
   rewrote film history
  </em>
 </a>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  .]
 </em>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 A longtime feminist, she was acutely sensitive to oppression and many of her movies center on women. In a 1975 interview with a French magazine, she said she didn’t have any more problems than her male cohort; making movies is difficult for everyone. At the same time, she was aware of her standing as the lone female director in the New Wave and how her presence could be used — as she put it — as an “
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  alibi
 </em>
 assuaging the consciences of the men.” But there were also rules. “As long as I was ‘little Varda,’ ‘little Agnès,’ the exception,” and she didn’t get in anyone’s way, she said, she was “helped out, supported and appreciated by my colleagues and companions.”
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Varda was in her mid-20s when she shot “La Pointe Courte” in 1954 and a photographer for the Theatre National Populaire in Paris. It was some years before she made her second feature, “Cléo From 5 to 7” (1962). In between those two movies, she made short films, had her first child (a daughter), met her husband,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.criterion.com/boxsets/1055-the-essential-jacques-demy" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  the director Jacques Demy
 </a>
 (a son followed much later), and traveled to China. “Cléo” was a great success, and she continued to make excitingly adventurous, insistently political work. Among the most unsettling is “Le Bonheur” (1965), a shocking, pitiless evisceration of the romantic vision of heterosexual marriage in which women are fundamentally disposable and replaceable.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “Le Bonheur” was criticized, including by those who wanted Varda to wag her finger at patriarchy and female oppression, doing the audience’s work for it. But hers is not a cinema of the obvious. One sustaining pleasure of her films is that they don’t neatly fit into boxes, including feminist ones, even while being unmistakably feminist. She wasn’t creating ideals or role models, but specific women in specific places (villages, streets, beaches) who are navigating a world in which the very definitions of women, of the feminine and of femininity are in upheaval. She was after the
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  contradictions
 </em>
 , a word that appears in many of her interviews, between clichés and “the images of lived life.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 This wasn’t necessarily what the industry was interested in, and sometimes Varda directed short movies “to keep alive in my own research” as she once put it. The latter-life acclaim and honors were overdue and welcome —
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.oscars.org/governors-awards/agnes-varda" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  she clearly enjoyed being feted
 </a>
 — but she didn’t always have institutional and critical support. In the early 1980s, she spoke about being omitted from history books and from special issues of Cahiers du Cinéma dedicated to French cinema. “I was just plain forgotten,” she said. She kept going even if, as she admitted in 1986, with each film she had “to fight like a tiger.”
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 That year, one of her most critically and commercially
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/1986/05/16/movies/screen-agnes-varda-s-vagabond.html?module=inline" title="a review">
  successful films, “Vagabond,”
 </a>
 about a dangerously unsettled woman, was released in the United States. I distinctly remember being freaked out by “Vagabond,” which opens on the corpse of Mona (a blazing Sandrine Bonnaire), a drifter who freezes to death in a country ditch. Raw, opaque yet also deeply moving, the film tracks her as she wanders from place to place, person to person, alone and finally unknowable. She’s a startlingly uncommon cinematic creation, partly because she is a woman who says no, including to other people.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Varda said she didn’t know why Mona repeatedly said no, a refusal that ends in death. I see “Vagabond,” in part, as a tough, unsentimental exploration about the limits of radical independence for women, which is perhaps what gives it the autobiographical aspect that runs through her movies. Varda’s life and work are filled with contrasts between interiority and exteriority, individuals and their worlds, which makes her house on Rue Daguerre, with its pink facade and striped doors, feel like another auteurist creation.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 For the past few years, my friend Joan Dupont and I would have an annual lunch with Varda at her home, eating and talking while periodically visited by one of her cats. She was predictably funny and warm and brilliant, but also sharp and strong and willful. During our lunch last year, Varda said she was tired. But she was as voluble as ever and filled with plans. She reminisced about her mother, who loved art, and her father, who didn’t, and spoke about growing old. “Never complain, never explain,” she said. She asked me about Ava DuVernay. The next week, Varda was off to the Cannes Film Festival, where she presented a restoration of her 1977 film “One Sings, the Other Doesn’t.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 At Cannes, she joined DuVernay and Cate Blanchett on the festival’s red carpet, this time to
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/05/12/movies/cannes-women-protest.html?module=inline" title="">
  protest gender inequality at the festival
 </a>
 . It was gratifying and deeply moving to see her among all these other women, though I wondered how many understood what Varda — through her example, her will and her art — had done to pave the way for them. In very concrete ways, in film after film, she helped show women that there was a place for them in movies beyond that of the inevitably beautiful, inevitably young star.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 At the end of “The Beaches of Agnès,” Varda asks, “What is cinema?” repeating André Bazin’s essential question. She is standing in one of the witty structures — a house of cinema — that she had fabricated for her multimedia installations. Cinema is light, images, dark, color, she says, over shots of the edifice with its metal frame and walls of film strips. Soon, she is inside. “In here, it feels like I live in cinema, cinema is my home,” she says. “I think I’ve always lived in it.” In the last shot, Varda is looking directly at the camera — at us — framed in an open door that feels like an invitation.
</p><p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>
<p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>
<p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>
<p>

 Supported by
</p><p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
  Noel Murray
 </span>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  Every month, Netflix Australia adds a new batch of movies and TV shows to its library. Here are our picks for April, broken down by release date.
 </em>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘Ultraman’
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming: April 1
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Along with “Astro Boy” and “Speed Racer,” the 1960s live-action superhero series “Ultraman” was one of the first Japanese TV programs to reach a mainstream audience worldwide. In its homeland, this tale of a gigantic armored alien and his symbiotic human companion has inspired sequels and spinoffs galore, including a current manga series that follows the adventures of the original hero’s son. That comic has been adapted to this new animated TV series, which blends traditional anime techniques with CGI.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘American Crime Story: The Assassination of Gianni Versace’
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  April 4
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Although it didn’t get anywhere near the buzz or ratings of season one’s “
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2016/02/02/arts/television/tv-review-people-vs-oj-simpson-american-crime-story.html?module=inline" title="">
  The People v. O.J. Simpson
 </a>
 ,” the “American Crime Story” second-season deep dive into the lives of Gianni Versace (played by Édgar Ramirez) and his murderer Andrew Cunanan (played by Darren Criss) is in a lot of ways even bolder and more inventive. The first episode begins with the serial killer gunning down the fashion designer, and then hour by hour “The Assassination of Gianni Versace” moves backward in the timeline, investigating how both these men were shaped by their family lives, by their egos and by the underground nature of gay culture in the late 20th century.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘Chilling Adventures of Sabrina’ Season 1, Part 2
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  April 5
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The special Christmas episode of “Chilling Adventures of Sabrina” that arrived in mid-December of 2018 offered a brief respite in the ongoing saga of the well-meaning teenage witch, Sabrina Spellman (played by Kiernan Shipka). But as part two of the series’ first season begins, the heroine finds herself back in a mess of her own making. She’s tapped into demonic powers to help her friends, and is now having trouble keeping her darker impulses in check. As with the season’s first half, expect story lines that evoke Harry Potter and “Buffy the Vampire Slayer,” with an eerily beautiful visual style that resembles “Rosemary’s Baby” and Hammer horror films.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘You vs. Wild’
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  April 10
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Continuing the “choose your own adventure” experiment of last year’s much-talked-about “Black Mirror” special episode “
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/01/04/arts/television/bandersnatch-black-mirror-netflix.html?module=inline" title="">
  Bandersnatch
 </a>
 ,” Netflix is now applying branching options to a Bear Grylls survival show. In “You vs. Wild,” the celebrity outdoorsman will once again brave extreme weather to have dangerous wildlife encounters. But in this new series, viewers will have opportunities throughout to choose the ways Grylls goes.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">

 </em>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/australia-letter?utm_source=ausmid&amp;module=inline" title="">
  <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
   Sign up for the weekly

 </a>
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/australia-letter?utm_source=ausmid&amp;module=inline">

   <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
    Australia Letter
   </em>
  </a>
 </a>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/australia-letter?utm_source=ausmid&amp;module=inline" title="">
  <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
   here
  </em>
 </a>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/signup/AUST?module=inline" title="">
  <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
   .
  </em>
 </a>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  ]
 </em>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘Black Summer’
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  April 11
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The horror-comedy series “
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2014/09/12/arts/television/z-nation-a-zombie-series-on-syfy.html?module=inline" title="">
  Z Nation
 </a>
 ” developed a small but fervent fan following over the course of its five seasons, telling strange, sardonic stories about how society might adjust to a zombie apocalypse. The prequel spinoff “Black Summer” circles back to the beginning of the outbreak, following a mother (played by Jaime King) who’s trying to find her way to her daughter, dodging the undead while forging relationships with other survivors who’ve been left behind. The new show is being pitched as a more traditional thriller, replacing wry humor with white-knuckle chases and narrow escapes.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘Street Food’
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  April 26
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The Netflix series “Chef’s Table” sets a new standard for how TV shows about cooking could look and feel, bringing psychological depth and visual lyricism to the subject of food. Some of the “Chef’s Table” creative team will now take that same approach to the world’s street vendors in “Street Food,” a series focusing on the culture of entire cities and countries — including Singapore and India, in season one — rather than on the habits and obsessions of individual chefs.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘She-Ra and the Princesses of Power’ Season 2
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  April 26
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The rebooted version of the clunky ‘80s syndicated cartoon “She-Ra: Princess of Power” was one of 2018’s most pleasant TV surprises: a fusion of the original’s old-fashioned science-fiction/fantasy mythology with the cultural diversity, emotional richness and character-driven storytelling of more modern animated series. Even the name was changed, to reflect a premise that surrounds the title heroine with multiple magical warriors. Having introduced a teeming cast of complicated heroes and villains in season one, the series’ creator Noelle Stevenson and her staff of writers and illustrators will in year two be looking to put them into action in stories with more epic sweep.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Also of Interest
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “Roman Empire: Caligula: The Mad Emperor” (April 5), “Tijuana” (April 5), “Huge in France” (April 12), “Special” (April 12), “My First First Love” (April 18), “Samantha!” Season 2 (April 19), “Selection Day: New Episodes” (April 22), “Bonding” (April 24), “Archer” Season 9 (April 26), “The Protector” Season 2 (April 26) and “Yankee” (April 26)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘Unicorn Store’
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming: April 5
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Brie Larson is having a stellar 2019 so far, first starring in the international box-office smash “Captain Marvel,” and now seeing her 2017 directorial debut “Unicorn Store” finally get distribution via Netflix. Written by Samantha McIntyre, “Unicorn Store” stars Larson as Kit, an aimless, artsy young woman, who’s just about to settle for a dull-but-stable life in a corporate cubicle when she gets an offer from a mysterious stranger (played by Samuel L. Jackson), who says he can sell her a unicorn. The film’s quirkily colorful images are in service of a whimsical story, meant to raise questions about what it means — realistically — to “follow your dream.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘La La Land’
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  April 9
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Can a movie that grossed over $400 million worldwide, and that
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/02/27/movies/oscars-best-picture-moonlight-academy-awards.html?action=click&amp;contentCollection=Music&amp;module=inline&amp;region=Body&amp;pgtype=article" title="">
  nearly won the Oscar for Best Picture
 </a>
 , be underrated? Now that the frenzy of the 2016 awards season has abated, it’s time to re-appreciate “La La Land,” the writer-director Damien Chazelle’s heartfelt and ambitious musical, about two aspiring Los Angeles artists — a jazz musician played by Ryan Gosling, and an actress played by Emma Stone — who help each other grow into the people they’re meant to be. Tuneful, stylish and masterfully acted, “La La Land” is a wonderful throwback to the best of classic Hollywood and the French New Wave.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘The Big Short’
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  April 7
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 After directing some of the best-loved comedies of the 21st century — including “Anchorman,” “Talladega Nights” and “Step Brothers” — Adam McKay tried something different with 2015’s “The Big Short,” an adaptation of Michael Lewis’ nonfiction book about the 2008 financial crisis. An all-star cast that includes Brad Pitt, Ryan Gosling, Steve Carell and an Oscar-nominated Christian Bale help McKay and his fellow screenwriter Charles Randolph tell a complicated story about the people who predicted the market crash, and who made a lot of money thanks to flaws in the system. This is a lively, frequently funny movie, with a point to make about how everyday folks are often lied to and betrayed by politicians and pundits.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘The Silence’
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  April 12
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 It’s hard to avoid comparing the horror film “The Silence” with the recent blockbuster “
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/04/04/movies/a-quiet-place-review-john-krasinski-emily-blunt.html?module=inline" title="">
  A Quiet Place
 </a>
 ,” given that both are about close-knit families surviving on a ravaged Earth, avoiding monsters who hunt their prey by sound. But “The Silence” is actually based on a Tim Lebbon novel, which predates “A Quiet Place.” Stanley Tucci plays the heroic patriarch, while Kiernan Shipka plays his daughter, whose other senses have been heightened since she lost her hearing at 13. The veteran horror cinematographer and director John R. Leonetti directs, from a script by Dick Van Dyke’s grandchildren, Carey and Shane Van Dyke.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘The Perfect Date’
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  April 12
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Netflix’s high school rom-com machine keeps rolling with “The Perfect Date,” which features a lead performance by
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/09/21/style/noah-centineo-to-all-the-boys-ive-loved-before.html?module=inline" title="">
  Noah Centineo
 </a>
 , who’s previously appeared in Netflix’s similar “To All the Boys I’ve Loved Before” and “Sierra Burgess Is a Loser.” Here he plays Brooks Rattigan, a broke student headed to college, who decides to make some extra money for school by renting himself out to young women who’d like to be seen with a smart, handsome lad. As tends to happen in these kinds of movies, Brooks soon finds that separating his business from his emotions proves difficult.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘Someone Great’
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  April 19
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The “Jane the Virgin” star Gina Rodriguez and the “Atlanta”/“Sorry to Bother You” actor Lakeith Stanfield play a newly broken-up couple in the writer-director Jennifer Kaytin Robinson’s “Someone Great,” a romantic comedy more about the bond between friends than about boy meets girl. When Rodriguez’s pop-culture reporter character Jenny gets a job at a magazine in San Francisco, she and her two best pals — played by DeWanda Wise and Brittany Snow — have one last wild romp together in New York City to help Jenny to get over her heartbreak and to bid her a spectacular farewell.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘Persona: Collection’
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  April 5
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The Korean pop singer Lee Ji-eun — sometimes professionally known as IU — plays four different characters in “Persona,” a collection of short films by up-and-coming Korean directors. In “Love Set,” “Collector,” “Kiss Burn” and “Walking at Night,” Lee plays the lead in everything from a tennis drama to a surreal romance, in stories that shift from light comedy to something more gripping. This project is a change of pace for the musician, who’s been a staple on television in her home country for years, but has less experience making movies.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Also of Interest
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “The Exorcist” (April 1), “Sense and Sensibility” (April 1), “Something’s Gotta Give” (April 1), “Suzzanna: Buried Alive” (April 3), “Our Planet” (April 5), “Who Would You Take to a Deserted Island?” (April 12), “American History X” (April 15), “Bridget Jones’s Diary” (April 17), “Bridget Jones: The Edge of Reason” (April 17), “Les Miserables” (April 17), “Savages” (April 17), “The World’s End” (April 17), “A Fortunate Man” (April 19), “Music Teacher” (April 19), “Grass is Greener” (April 20), “Aurora” (April 25) and “The Nice Guys” (April 30)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘I Think You Should Leave with Tim Robinson’
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  April 23
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The loud-voiced comic Tim Robinson is probably best-known for cocreating and co-starring in the sweetly eccentric sitcom “Detroiters,” with his friend Sam Richardson. But he also spent a few years as a writer and an occasional bit player on “Saturday Night Live.” Robinson puts that sketch training to use — along with his ability to make a strong, not always positive first impression — in the offbeat cringe-comedy show “I Think You Should Leave.” In a variety of scenarios, Robinson and others play characters so obnoxious and intense that they drive anyone in the immediate vicinity into fleeing.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 —
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Also of Interest
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “Kevin Hart: Irresponsible” (April 2) and “Anthony Jeselnik: Fire in the Maternity Ward” (April 30)
