<p class="g-body">
 We’ve seen countless stories of nasty, selfish people who go on a voyage of self-discovery and come out the other side as better, wiser souls. This acidic comedy-drama asks: What if that journey didn’t take? Mavis Gary (
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2011/10/30/movies/charlize-theron-strong-enough-to-play-the-heavy.html">
  Charlize Theron
 </a>
 , in take-no-prisoners mode), a bitter young-adult author who returns to her hometown in hopes of reuniting with her high-school boyfriend, his picture-perfect married life be damned. A film that zigs when you’re certain it will zag, “Young Adult” tells a satisfying story that is also a sly critique of the conventions of modern moviemaking. Our critic praised its “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2011/12/09/movies/charlize-theron-in-young-adult-review.html">

 </a>

<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/h7defXf" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 In 1989,
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/topic/subject/central-park-jogger-case-1989">
  the country was shocked
 </a>
 by the sexual assault and near-death of a young white jogger in Central Park. Five black and Latino youths were quickly charged, tried, sentenced and imprisoned — until a serial rapist
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2002/12/20/nyregion/convictions-and-charges-voided-in-89-central-park-jogger-attack.html?pagewanted=all&amp;src=pm">
  confessed
 </a>
 over a decade later, his claim borne out by DNA evidence. This informative and infuriating documentary by Ken Burns,
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2012/11/18/movies/sarah-burns-and-the-documentary-central-park-five.html">
  Sarah Burns
 </a>
 and David McMahon meticulously details the charged atmosphere in which the five teenagers were accused and convicted, as well as the tremendous personal toll taken by this miscarriage of justice. Our critic called it “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2012/11/22/movies/the-documentary-the-central-park-five.html">
  emotionally stirring
 </a>
 .”
</p>
<p class="g-body">
</p>
<p class="g-body">
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/iiy3W1J" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 Michelle Pfeiffer finally found her star-making role in this deliriously enjoyable gangster comedy from the director Jonathan Demme (“The Silence of the Lambs”). She plays Angela de Marco, a Mob widow who finds herself caught between the crime family of her dead husband (Alec Baldwin) and the affable F.B.I. man (Matthew Modine) who wants her to work for him. Jazzily mounted and giddily funny, our critic called it “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/1988/08/19/movies/review-film-the-mob-to-have-and-to-hold.html">
  wildly overdecorated screwball farce
 </a>
 .” (For more modern screwball fun, stream “
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/8d2wDup">
   The Adventures of Priscilla, Queen of the Desert
  </a>
 </strong>
 ” or “
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/8DEEXBW">
   Dirty Rotten Scoundrels
  </a>
 </strong>
 ” on Prime.)
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/8bJ6kjM" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 Over the course of this wistful and lovely low-key dramedy from Jim Jarmusch, the bus-driving poet named Paterson (Adam Driver) does not seek success, discovery or even publication. That’s not why he writes — it’s about routine and release. Intoxicatingly lived-in, “Paterson” is a valentine to all of those who create art not to make a living, but to sustain their souls in the meantime. Our critic praised its “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2016/12/27/movies/paterson-review.html">
  visual precision and emotional restraint
 </a>
 .”
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/eFdu8zQ" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 Set at a group home for troubled teens, this 2013 indie drama from director Destin Daniel Cretton casts aside the after school-special conventions typical of such stories and digs out the dramatic truths buried within. Cretton offsets the inherently downbeat subject matter with an exuberant directorial hand and coaxes gutsy performances from his ace cast, including “before they were stars” turns by
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2013/08/18/movies/brie-larson-talks-about-short-term-12.html">
  Brie Larson
 </a>
 , Lakeith Stanfield, Stephanie Beatriz and Rami Malek. Our critic noted, “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2013/08/23/movies/short-term-12-delves-into-life-at-a-group-home.html">
  Mr. Cretton manages to earn your tears honestly
 </a>
 .” (Admirers of indie coming-of-age dramas should also check out “
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/5Hud8Bd">
   Mud
  </a>
 </strong>
 ” and “
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/5Z0GVxh">
   Precious
  </a>
 </strong>
 .”)
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/dEl9iXO" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 Charles Chaplin’s first feature-length comedy — “six reels of joy,” according to the original advertisements — was informed by his suspicion that audiences would grow restless if subjected to an hour-plus of gags and slapstick. So he went all-in on pathos, creating a story in which his iconic Little Tramp character discovers an abandoned baby, raises the child as his own and must then summon all his ingenuity to keep their makeshift family intact. Even this first time out, Chaplin juggles the seemingly incongruent tones with ease. Our critic praised Chaplin’s “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/1921/01/22/archives/the-screen.html">
  inimitable pantomime
 </a>
 .” (Silent movie fanatics will also want to stream the Amazon original “
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/h9Rdw8w">
   Wonderstruck
  </a>
 </strong>
 .”)
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/cc2C6Xd" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 Emily V. Gordon and Kumail Nanjiani based their first screenplay on their own, unconventional love story — a courtship that was paused, then oddly amplified by an unexpected illness and a medically induced coma. This isn’t typical rom-com fodder, but it’s written and played with such honesty and heart that it somehow lands. Nanjiani and Zoe Kazan (standing in for Gordon) generate easy, lived-in chemistry and a rooting interest in the relationship, while a second-act appearance by Holly Hunter and Ray Romano as her parents creates a prickly tension that gives way to hard-won affection. Our critic deemed it “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/06/22/movies/the-big-sick-review-kumail-nanjiani.html">
  a joyous, generous-hearted romantic comedy
 </a>
 .” (If you like your comedies with a dash of heartfelt drama, we recommend “
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/dBciJ78">
   Moonstruck
  </a>
 </strong>
 ” and“
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/d07ldTg">
   Jeff, Who Lives at Home
  </a>
 </strong>
 .”)
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/1NFNngX" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 As director of the
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2007/06/08/movies/08ocea.html">
  “Ocean’s” trilogy
 </a>
 ,
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/08/10/movies/logan-lucky-steven-soderbergh-interview.html">
  Steven Soderbergh
 </a>
 honored the classic heist movie aesthetic: sleek, classy and star-studded. And then he set out to subvert all of those conventions with this working-class heist comedy, in which a minor character describes its central job as “Ocean’s 7-11.” The key players are familiar (the safecracker, the computer whiz, the sexy girl, the brains of the operation), but they’re done with salty fun and earthy humor. You’ll never say “cauliflower” the same way again. Our critic dubbed it “gravity-defying” and “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/08/16/movies/review-logan-lucky-steven-soderbergh-and-his-motley-band-of-thieves.html">
  ridiculously entertaining
 </a>
 .” (Caper movie fans may also enjoy the somewhat grittier “
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/8C2gOoq">
   A Simple Plan
  </a>
 </strong>
 ” and “
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/gO6ORSr">
   Good Time
  </a>
  .
 </strong>
 ”)
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/3dVRcum" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 Osgood Perkins — son of the “Psycho” star Anthony Perkins — writes and directs this unnerving and disturbing story of creepy goings-on at a near-empty girls’ boarding school. The performances (from Kiernan Shipka, Lucy Boynton, and Emma Roberts) are sharp and the scares are genuine, while Perkins’ orchestration of mood and atmosphere is chillingly effective. Our critic called it “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/03/30/movies/the-blackcoats-daughter-review.html">
  perfectly acted and gorgeously filmed
 </a>
 .”
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/2yOmBuI" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 Kevin Costner, Susan Sarandon and Tim Robbins were all propelled to the next level of stardom by this 1988 sleeper hit from the writer-director Ron Shelton, and it’s not hard to see why. It’s a laid-back charmer, endlessly funny and casually sexy, and it gives all of them the opportunity to do what they do best: it features Costner shooting straight, Sarandon smoldering, and Robbins playing an amiable goofball. Our critic praised its “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/1988/06/15/movies/review-film-when-baseball-is-love-poetry-and-even-religion.html">
  spirit and sex appeal
 </a>
 .” (Sarandon also stars in “
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/cB9Irfr">
   Thelma and Louise
  </a>
 </strong>
 ,” currently streaming on Prime.)
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/1XG95qR" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 The first two collaborations between Clint Eastwood and director Sergio Leone, “A Fistful of Dollars” and “A Few Dollars More,” did nothing less than reinvent a genre, diverting popular attention from the increasingly stodgy traditional Western to the so-called “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2005/07/30/movies/MoviesFeatures/saluting-father-of-spaghetti-westerns.html">
  Spaghetti Western
 </a>
 ,” which ramped up the bloodshed, self-awareness and stylistic exuberance. Those films were modest, low-budget affairs, but Leone and Eastwood broke the mold with this trilogy-ending masterpiece in 1966, which runs nearly three hours and elevates its antiheroes to near-mythic status. Our critic called it “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2003/05/25/movies/film-sergio-leone-s-jazz-western.html">
  luridly intoxicating
 </a>
 .” (“
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/1aZu6jU">
   A Fistful of Dollars
  </a>
 </strong>
 ”and “
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/2izspoc">
   For a Few Dollars More
  </a>
 </strong>
 ” are also streaming on Prime.)
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/gaoVqO7" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 The original 1956 “Invasion of the Body Snatchers,” in which alien invaders implant themselves in humans and take on their form, was widely seen as an allegory for the Red Scare. This “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/1978/12/22/archives/screen-body-snatchers-return-in-all-their-creepy-glory.html">
  dazzling remake
 </a>
 ,” as our critic described it, is updated and released from that context, but it found another in post-hippie, health-obsessed San Francisco. The stakes are lower, but the remake has a self-aware sense of humor and a decent proportion of gross-outs and jump-scares, as well as an ending that’s just as creepy as the original’s. (Fans of trippy thrillers will also enjoy “
 <a href="http://a.co/d/4a4Fxfh">
  Always Shine
 </a>
 .”)
</p>
<p class="g-body">
</p>
<p class="g-body">
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/46B4lXI" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 The esteemed character actor Charles Laughton made his one and only trip behind the camera for this haunting small-town thriller. Robert Mitchum crafts a chilling, unforgettable performance as Harry Powell, a mysterious stranger who romances a widowed mother (a superb Shelley Winters) whose children seem to be the only ones capable of seeing the evil within him. Our critic called it “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/1955/09/30/archives/bogeyman-plus.html">
  clever and exceptionally effective
 </a>
 .” (Classic movie fans will also want to stream David Lean’s 1946 adaptation of “
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/bnARebk">
   Great Expectations
  </a>
 </strong>
 .”)
</p>
<p class="g-body">
</p>
<p class="g-body">
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/8mS1Gzr" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 John Belushi and Dan Aykroyd kicked off the tradition of translating “Saturday Night Live” characters to the big screen with this outsized musical-action-comedy from the director John Landis (“Animal House”). The plot is old hat: The title characters try to put their old band back together in order to save their childhood orphanage. But it proves to be an ingenious clothesline upon which Aykroyd and Landis’s script can hang a serious of rousing musical numbers and car-crunching chase scenes. Our critic called those sequences “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2011/07/31/movies/homevideo/blu-ray-releases-animal-house-and-blues-brothers.html">
  dazzling
 </a>
 .” (For more irreverent musical comedy, check out “
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/gJ6JCZx">
   A Funny Thing Happened on the Way to the Forum
  </a>
 </strong>
 .”)
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/e9gR1yr" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 The director John Schlesinger captures the sights and sounds (and practically the smells) of Times Square in the late 1960s with this absorbing winner of the Oscar for Best Picture — the first and only X-rated movie to capture that prize. Jon Voight was propelled to stardom by his charming performance as Joe Buck, a naïve Texas boy who comes to New York City with visions of rich women in his head; Dustin Hoffman created another memorable character as the street-wise native who shows him the ropes. (“I’m walkin’ here!”) Our critic called it “
 <a href="https://archive.nytimes.com/www.nytimes.com/packages/html/movies/bestpictures/cowboy-re.html?scp=1&amp;sq=frank%20buck&amp;st=cse">
  a moving experience
 </a>
 that captures the quality of a time and a place.”
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/isHOlLO" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 This Polish possession story from the writer and director Marcin Wrona opens on a note of uncertainty and dread and then holds it for 94 harrowing minutes. Wrona transforms the relatable fears of wedding day into something far more sinister, as our groom protagonist discovers horrifying skeletons in his new family’s closet (or, more accurately, its yard); the filmmaker offsets the considerable nightmare imagery and wild-eyed desperation with piercing moments of gallows humor, particularly in contemplating how “sensible people” might react to these events. Our critic praised its “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2016/09/09/movies/demon-review.html">
  light shivers
 </a>
 ” and “bluntly old-fashioned screen magic.”
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/4sJY0xI" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>
<p class="g-body">
 Oliver Stone graduated from a respected screenwriter to a top-flight filmmaker with this harrowing
 <a href="https://archive.nytimes.com/www.nytimes.com/packages/html/movies/bestpictures/platoon-ar4.html?scp=3&amp;sq=prism&amp;st=cse">
  Vietnam War drama
 </a>
 , which won Oscars for best Picture and director. Stone based the film on his own experiences in Vietnam, with Charlie Sheen as his avatar, a clean-cut kid from a privileged background whose eyes are opened to the horrors of combat and conflict. Willem Dafoe and Tom Berenger singe the screen as his sergeants, one free-spirited and open, the other hard-edged and cruel. Our critic called it a “
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/1986/12/19/movies/film-the-vietnam-war-in-stone-s-platoon.html">
  vivid, terse, exceptionally moving
 </a>
 ” film. (For a more conventional war movie, queue up “
 <strong>
  <a href="http://a.co/d/1m2FUEI">
   The Great Escape
  </a>
 </strong>
 .”)
</p>
<p class="movie__link g-body">
 <a href="http://a.co/d/gaNDKh7" target="_blank">
  Watch on Amazon
 </a>
</p>

</p><p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
  Gabe Cohn
 </span>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  Sidney Lumet’s “Network” is on TCM. And it’s a night of Bourne and Bond.
 </em>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  NETWORK
 </strong>
 (1976)
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  10:15 p.m. on TCM.
 </em>
 A recent
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/11/21/theater/network-broadway-bryan-cranston.html?module=inline" title="">
  staged reimagining
 </a>
 has given this Paddy Chayefsky satire of TV-obsessed America
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/06/theater/network-review-bryan-cranston.html?module=inline" title="">
  a new life on Broadway
 </a>
 — but that doesn’t mean the original movie version has lost its bite. Directed by Sidney Lumet, the film follows Howard Beale (Peter Finch), a land mine of a news anchor who, after learning that he is to be fired, goes on live TV and tells the county that he plans to commit suicide on air. He doesn’t do it, but the episode garners high ratings, saving Beale’s job and shaping the tenor of his subsequent newscasts, which become increasingly intense. Faye Dunaway plays a network executive. Dunaway and Finch won Oscars for their performances, as did Beatrice Straight, for a supporting role, and Chayefsky, for his screenplay. The film now runs alongside the very kinds of programming it seeks to skewer.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  JASON BOURNE
 </strong>
 (2016)
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  7:44 p.m. and 10:04 p.m. on FXM.
 </em>
 Throughout the 2000s, Matt Damon’s amnesiac ex-C.I.A. operative Jason Bourne captured attention with few words. The cold, often intimate violence dispatched by Damon’s quiet antihero in “The Bourne Identity” (2002), “The Bourne Supremacy” (2004) and “The Bourne Ultimatum” (2007) helped define action movies of the era. After nine years off, Damon
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2016/07/17/movies/matt-damon-paul-greengrass-jason-bourne.html?module=inline" title="">
  returned in this continuation of the series
 </a>
 . Its plot weaves in some global concerns from the time Bourne was away, including hacktivism, the growing influence of social media companies and mass surveillance.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  DIE ANOTHER DAY
 </strong>
 (2002)
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  Stream on
 </em>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.netflix.com/title/60024927" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
   Netflix
  </em>
 </a>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  .
 </em>
 The same year Jason Bourne arrived on the spy-movie scene to ramp up the violence and pare down excesses of the genre, Pierce Brosnan was firing rockets from an invisible car and fencing with an instructor played by Madonna in this, his final turn as James Bond. From its opening hovercraft chase to its grand-finale fight aboard an airplane, “Die Another Day” is about as over the top as possible. Halle Berry memorably stars opposite Brosnan as an N.S.A. agent. If this movie is too extravagant for your tastes, but you want more wisecracks than “Jason Bourne” makes, consider the decidedly darker
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  CASINO ROYALE
 </strong>
 (2006), at 8 p.m. on Paramount Network; it features a Bond (Daniel Craig) for a post-Bourne world.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  TURN UP CHARLIE
 </strong>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  Stream on
 </em>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.netflix.com/title/80124723" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
   Netflix
  </em>
 </a>
 . The English actor and
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=qvQ3ugT4uLo" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  actual D.J.
 </a>
 Idris Elba plays a washed-up English D.J. turned nanny in this new series. His character is recruited to look after Gabrielle (Frankie Hervey), an 11-year-old troublemaker. That unlikely pairing becomes a source of both laughs and sentimentality, Mike Hale wrote in
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/03/15/arts/television/turn-up-charlie-review-idris-elba-netflix.html?module=inline" title="">
  his review for The New York Times
 </a>
 . Hale noted that the show mixed “lachrymose family drama in with the odd-couple farce.” But, he added, “if you can live without seeing Idris Elba saying ‘I’m adorbs’ to an 11-year-old girl, you can live without ‘Turn Up Charlie.’”
</p><p>

 Supported by
</p><p class="css-c2jxua e6idgb70">
 Critics’ Notebook
</p>
<p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <a class="css-1riqqik e1jsehar0" href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/manohla-dargis">
  <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
   Manohla Dargis
  </span>
 </a>
 and
 <a class="css-1riqqik e1jsehar0" href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/a-o--scott">
  <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
   A.O. Scott
  </span>
 </a>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Every spring, the Museum of Modern Art and the Film Society of Lincoln Center present New Yorkers with the cinematic miscellany known as New Directors/New Films. This year’s program of movies, many gleaned from the international festival circuit, runs the gamut from the glorious to the grim and includes works of socially conscious realism, historical fantasy and experimental documentary. Our chief film critics, Manohla Dargis and A.O. Scott, offer these highlights.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Some time in the 18th century, a young African boy is purchased by a European noblewoman and subjected to a curious form of enslavement. Given the name Angelo, he grows up among aristocrats who treat him not as a servant but as a curio and a symbol of their own benevolence. Angelo’s biography feels like both a plausibly factual chronicle and a fantastical allegory. Shot with classical rigor and attention to detail (with room for a few telling anachronisms), the Austrian filmmaker Markus Schleinzer’s second feature presents history as a lavish, lucid nightmare from which nobody, Angelo least of all, can hope to awaken. (A.O. Scott)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 A tale twice told, the ingenious Turkish puzzle “Belonging” opens with an unseen narrator introducing the story and then another voice delivering a just-the-facts description of a murder. Detailed, largely affectless and sometimes rushed, this recitation accompanies images that initially seem completely unrelated, even random. In time, though, words and images begin syncing up — we hear “the security chain was locked” over a shot of a safety chain lock — creating a seamless correspondence between what we hear and see. The writer-director Burak Cevik then flips the switch and the movie shifts into a more lyrical narrative register, one that fills in all the little nuances, most notably the intimate in-between moments that both explain and obscure so much. (Manohla Dargis)
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://variety.com/2018/film/festivals/mexican-revolution-lila-aviles-1202992396/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Lila Avilés’s debut feature
 </a>
 never leaves the high-rise Mexico City hotel where its title character, Eve (Gabriela Cartol), works long hours cleaning rooms with spectacular views. The effect of is claustrophobic, but also strangely serene, even sublime. The camera follows Eve through daily routines that include awkward encounters with guests, friendly exchanges with co-workers and curt meetings with supervisors. Our sense of exploitation and alienation is palpable, but the moments of beauty, tenderness and freedom that punctuate the drudgery provide flickers of humanity that feel almost miraculous. (A.O.S.)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Chinonye Chukwu’s film, which opens New Directors, is a somber, ethically serious
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.indiewire.com/2019/02/sundance-chinonye-chukwu-first-black-woman-grand-jury-prize-1202040823/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  consideration of the death penalty
 </a>
 . It’s an issue movie that wants to be thought-provoking rather than polemical. It is also a showcase for the formidable talents of Alfre Woodard, who plays the warden of a prison where executions are a regular part of life. As the next one approaches (the condemned man is played by Aldis Hodge) the warden struggles with emotions that threaten every aspect of her identity, professional and personal. Woodard enacts this struggle with minimal vanity and abundant grace. (A.O.S.)
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 In 2004, María Alché appeared in “The Holy Girl,” the Argentine director Lucrecia Martel’s haunting second feature. Fifteen years later,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://variety.com/2018/film/festivals/locarno-maria-alche-on-familia-sumergida-and-an-excess-of-light-1202897546/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Alché’s directing debut
 </a>
 shows signs of Martel’s influence in its blend of oblique narration and subtle psychological insight. But Alché, who is also an accomplished photographer, brings her own arresting visual sensibility to this story of grief, longing and memory. Mercedes Moran plays a middle-aged mother of three almost-grown children who is thrown off balance by the death of her sister. During her mourning period, nothing much happens, and yet everything happens, as Alché and Moran practice a kind of emotional sonar, picking up signals that lie deep under the dramatic surface. (A.O.S.)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The documentary “Honeyland” contains worlds in one beautiful, seemingly simple story. Its focus, Hatidze Muratova, lives in a tiny stone house in remote northern Macedonia with her octogenarian mother, a plucky dog and an irregular number of cats. Her passion and apparent livelihood, though, are the wild bees she tends —
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  keeping
 </em>
 is too possessive — with hand flaps, vocalizations and deep respect. The directors Ljubomir Stefanov and Tamara Kotevska don’t overexplain this world, its history or seemingly fragile present, including the origin of the chaotic, sprawling family who soon moves in and begins taking over the bees. The family’s at times infuriating disruptions (animal lovers beware) turn this quiet, moving observational documentary into a heart-skipping thriller as well as a perfect encapsulation of humanity’s catastrophic domination of nature. (M.D.)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 During the Kosovo war in 1999, against a backdrop of NATO bombardment, a Serbian truck driver (Leon Lucev), transports a mysterious cargo toward Belgrade. He gives a ride to a young man whose reasons for traveling are equally enigmatic, and the two of them make their way across a chilly, drab landscape in this mordant, minimalist road picture, directed by Ognjen Glavonic, who leavens the journey with hints of suspense and glints of absurdist humor. This isn’t “The Wages of Fear” so much as the dividends of existential anxiety and political despair. (A.O.S.)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 If it bleeds, it leads — and pays the bills. That’s the uneasy truism and slow-boiling moral of the gripping documentary “Midnight Family,” about a household of ambulance workers. The title isn’t metaphoric (or not exactly), but refers to the Ochoas, who operate one of the many private ambulances that race through Mexico City. Fantastically shot by the director Luke Lorentzen, the documentary develops an urgency that suits the life-or-death stakes onscreen. By turns terrifying and exhilarating, “Midnight Family” unfolds with such velocity that it may take a while for your ethical doubts to catch up to what’s happening. When they do, they leave you gasping. (M.D.)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The pig’s head on a stick, posted in a jungle encampment occupied by a pack of feral children, pays obvious homage to “Lord of the Flies.” But Alejandro Landes’s “Monos” infuses the themes of that schoolboy parable with a grim, contemporary political perspective and filters them through cinematic influences that include Terrence Malick and Werner Herzog. The kids are members of a guerrilla army in the Colombian countryside. Their mission is to guard an American hostage played by Julianne Nicholson. A lot goes wrong. As the action shifts from mountains to rain forest, Landes makes clever use of drone-mounted cameras and trippy sound design to turn the natural world into a hallucination. Unless the human characters are figments dreamed up by the landscape. (A.O.S.)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 When 16-year-old Mandy (an appealing
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.screendaily.com/features/stars-of-tomorrow-2018-rhianne-barreto-actor/5133192.article" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Rhianne Barreto
 </a>
 ) wakes up on her family’s front lawn one dark day, she finds herself with no memory of and a body marred with wounds. The question of what happened — as well as why, who and where — reverberates through this thoughtful, low-key drama which tracks Mandy’s anguish and anger as a personal trauma increasingly becomes an exploitative public spectacle. As Mandy fights to recover her memory, she struggles with her fears, family and friends, growing progressively more isolated. Making a fine feature debut, the American Pippa Bianco uses a depressingly familiar story of high-school partying gone wrong to explore sometimes uncomfortable questions about gender, agency and collective guilt. (M.D.)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Time bends in this sly, often-lovely drama, which pivots on a young surveyor whose past catches up with him while he’s out in the field. At one point, he walks into a seemingly deserted classroom, initiating an enigmatic journey into the past that keeps bumping into the present. Using splashes of primary color, punctuating zooms, visual echoes and narrative ellipses, the Chinese writer-director Qiu Sheng puts a fresh gloss on Faulkner’s observation that the past is never dead. It’s always here, vibrating in every red scarf, bird song and moment. “Suburban Birds” will open soon, but is so good — and delightfully kinked — that it’s well worth catching now (and watching twice). (M.D.)
</p><p>
 More information is at
 <a href="http://www.newdirectors.org/">
  newdirectors.org
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>

 </span>

 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
   Watching is The New York Times’s TV and film recommendation website. Sign up for our
  </em>
 </strong>
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/watching?module=inline">
  <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
   <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/watching" title="">
    twice-weekly newsletter here
   </a>
  </em>

 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">

   .
  </em>
 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  April is the cruelest month, save for when it comes to streaming selections. (And as an April bonus, we have an extra recommendation for you: Jordan Peele’s reboot of “
 </em>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.cbs.com/shows/the-twilight-zone/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
   The Twilight Zone
  </em>
 </a>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  ” on CBS All Access. The first two episodes will be available April 1.) Below are the most interesting of what we’ve found among the new TV series and movies coming to the major streaming services this month, plus a roundup of all the best new titles in all genres. (Streaming services occasionally change schedules without giving notice.)
 </em>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘
 </strong>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.netflix.com/search?q=Chilling%20Adventures%20of%20Sabrina&amp;jbv=80223989&amp;jbp=0&amp;jbr=0" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
   Chilling Adventures of Sabrina
  </strong>
 </a>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ’ Part 2
 </strong>
 <br/>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 April 5
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 When we last left Sabrina, she signed her name in the Book of the Beast. Now what? As we enter Season 2 — or Part 2, as Netflix likes to call it; or Phase 2, as the Dark Lord himself might dub his diabolic plan for our favorite teenage witch — Sabrina is starting to raise a little hell. Her devotion to the Dark Lord and her aptitude for magic itself are both put to the test when she confronts the inherent misogyny of the witch world. (Why is everything tipped in favor of the warlocks?) There are some clever horror in-jokes this season, and some spot-on guest stars familiar to the genre (Veronica Cartwright, Ray Wise). So tune in for more ominous intrigue, sexy interludes with the studly Nick Scratch and some facetime with Satan himself. Let the half-breed ascend!
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘
 </strong>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.netflix.com/title/80049832" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
   Our Planet
  </strong>
 </a>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ’ Season 1
 </strong>
 <br/>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 April 5
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 There is something beautifully soothing — and tragic, too — about the remarkable imagery on display in this globe-spanning wildlife series, which captures rare animals in their remote habitats. Thousands of pink flamingos, for example, take over a newly formed lake in an African salt pan after a torrential rain, a place where they can breed safely, away from predators. It’s a delight to see the flamingo chicks learning to walk out of there — until the focus shifts to the one that can’t keep up because the salt has solidified around its tiny limbs like crusty leg warmers. The narrator David Attenborough’s voice is warmly reassuring, even when telling us that wildlife populations are in decline. “For the first time in human history,” he says, “the stability of nature can no longer be taken for granted.” Here’s a chance to see it while you can.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘
 </strong>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.netflix.com/title/80202920" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
   Someone Great
  </strong>
 </a>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ’
 </strong>
 <br/>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 April 19
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 This sweet, funny movie raises an unavoidable question for the writer-director Jennifer Kaytin Robinson: Why would present-day New York music journalist Jenny (Gina Rodriguez) want to move to San Francisco to work for Rolling Stone, when Rolling Stone hasn’t been headquartered in San Francisco for more than 40 years? If you can set that aside, the rest of Jenny’s story might draw you in. The move to the West Coast brings a breakup with her boyfriend Nate (Lakeith Stanfield), and a heartbroken Jenny and her two BFFs (DeWanda Wise, Brittany Snow) decide to commiserate by spending the day (and night) playing hooky, getting high, and generally not acting their age. This is a celebration of female friendship and a bittersweet contemplation of receding youth, as Jenny is compelled to say goodbye to New York, goodbye to her twenties and of course, goodbye to Nate, with his limpid, soulful eyes.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘
 </strong>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.netflix.com/title/80210361" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
   Chambers
  </strong>
 </a>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ’
 </strong>
 <br/>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 April 26
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Two grieving parents (Uma Thurman, Tony Goldwyn) track down the teenage girl who was the recipient of their deceased daughter’s heart and begin generously giving her more things — fine clothes, a car, a full scholarship to a pricey private school. But the girl, Sasha (actress Sivan Alyra Rose), is a little weirded out by the parents’ unsolicited beneficence. And as she becomes further enmeshed in the life her young donor left behind, strange things begin happening. She becomes ambidextrous. She hears The Stone Roses playing when the radio’s not on. She has sudden violent impulses. Set against a Southwestern landscape, this horror series is disturbing, addictive and moody.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Also arriving:
 </strong>
 “All the President’s Men” (April 1), “Bonnie and Clyde” (April 1), “Deliverance” (April 1), “The Fifth Element” (April 1), “The Golden Compass” (April 1), “I Am Legend” (April 1), “Pineapple Express” (April 1), “P.S. I Love You” (April 1), “Snatch” (April 1), “Kevin Hart: Irresponsible” (April 2), “New Girl” Season 7 (April 10), “You vs. Wild” (April 10), “The Perfect Date” (April 12), “The Silence” (April 12), “I Think You Should Leave with Tim Robinson” Season 1 (April 23), “The Hateful Eight” (April 25), “American Honey” (April 27), “The Imitation Game” (April 29) and “Anthony Jeselnik: Fire in the Maternity Ward” (April 30).
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘
 </strong>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.amazon.com/Season-2-Official-Trailer/dp/B07P8X7ZBX/ref=sr_1_3?keywords=The+Tick&amp;qid=1553697139&amp;s=gateway&amp;sr=8-3" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
   The Tick
  </strong>
 </a>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ’ Season 2
 </strong>
 <br/>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 April 5
 <br/>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Last season’s nemesis, The Terror, has been rooted out and our unlikely blue do-gooder The Tick is back for another round of hyper-stylized superhero satire. This time he’s tackling government lies, human trafficking and other things of a murder-y nature. The Tick is so busy that his sidekick, Arthur, however, is having serious trouble keeping up. Arthur has poor danger reflexes, secret identity issues and a new nemesis called Lobstercules. There are also other heroes on hand (for team-ups) and several new villains. Haven’t seen Season 1, you say? No problem — this second outing is super easy to follow.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Also arriving:
 </strong>
 “The Addams Family” (April 1), “Beetlejuice” (April 1), “Bill &amp; Ted’s Excellent Adventure” (April 1), “Blade” (April 1), “Chinatown” (April 1), “Days of Thunder” (April 1), “Everything Must Go” (April 1), “In a World … ” (April 1), “Interview with the Vampire” (April 1), “The Minus Man” (April 1), “Murder She Wrote” Seasons 1-5 (April 1), “The Perfect Storm” (April 1), “Primal Fear” (April 1), “The Shawshank Redemption” (April 1), “Six Degrees of Separation” (April 1), “Stories We Tell” (April 1), “Top Gun” (April 1), “Up in the Air” (April 1), “A Quiet Place” (April 2), “Bosch” Season 5 (April 19), “Humans” Season 3 (April 25), “The Hole in the Ground” (April 27) and “Waiting for ‘Superman’” (April 29).
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘
 </strong>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://play.hbogo.com/series/urn:hbo:series:GWrpNUQ8IrsLCwwEAAAAC" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
   Wyatt Cenac’s Problem Areas
  </strong>
 </a>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ’ Season 2 Premiere
 </strong>
 <br/>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 April 5
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The new season of Wyatt Cenac’s docu-series focuses on the American education system and why it seems so broken. (And with the recent admissions scandals, he drolly notes, even bribing your child’s way into an education appears to be broken, too.) Cenac talks to students, educators, historians and others about issues like school unionization, the undervaluing of teachers and the school-to-prison pipeline. The series isn’t all hard slogging, though; Cenac also explores things like goat yoga classes, which can only be called amusing.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘
 </strong>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.hbo.com/movies/native-son" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
   Native Son
  </strong>
 </a>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ’
 </strong>
 <br/>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 April 6
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Richard Wright’s celebrated 1940 novel receives its third screen adaptation by the artist and first-time director Rashid Johnson. Wright’s story has been updated (by the Pulitzer Prize-winning playwright Suzan-Lori Parks), but his protagonist Bigger Thomas (Ashton Sanders, so good in “Moonlight”), is still a young black man trapped in a world rigged against him. Bigger rejects the gangster lifestyle offered by a friend and thinks he’s found a way out of poverty in the South Side of Chicago when he gets a job as a driver for a wealthy businessman, Henry Dalton (Bill Camp). But his dreams of self-improvement are doomed by his interactions with Dalton’s “woke” daughter Mary (Margaret Qualley), who is enamored by what she thinks Bigger represents. The movie isn’t a masterpiece, but it has much to admire: striking visual compositions, Sanders’s nuanced performance and a tense atmosphere which suggests much more than is said here.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘
 </strong>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://play.hbogo.com/series/urn:hbo:series:GVU2cggagzYNJjhsJATwo" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
   Game of Thrones
  </strong>
 </a>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ’ Season 8 Premiere
 </strong>
 <br/>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 April 14
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Some say Westeros will end in fire, some say in ice. One thing is for sure: “
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2019/arts/game-of-thrones.html?module=inline" title="">
  Game of Thrones
 </a>
 ” is ending this year. Yes, very, very sadly, our time with the secret prince, the dragon queen, the fiery witch, the tree-wizard and countless other characters is coming to a close (like the lives of so many other characters we’ve long missed). Will the living triumph over the army of the dead? Or will the Night King kill them all, and leave the world an icy hell? Oh, we (
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  sob
 </em>
 ) can’t go on …
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  [
 </em>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/watching-presents-your-game-of-thrones-guide?module=inline" title="">
  <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
   Rewatch “Game of Thrones” with us. Sign up to get our guides to the first seven seasons straight to your inbox.
  </em>
 </a>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  ]
 </em>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘
 </strong>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.hbo.com/gentleman-jack" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
   Gentleman Jack
  </strong>
 </a>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ’ Season 1 Premiere
 </strong>
 <br/>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 April 22
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 It’s 1832 in Halifax, West Yorkshire, England, and Anne Lister is determined to do whatever she very well pleases with her life. Driving a stagecoach, for example, or mercy-killing a horse — or dressing in masculine clothing and sleeping with women. Possibly even marrying one! Lister, a real-life historical figure, is widely considered to be the first modern lesbian, and this Anglo-American series, created by Sally Wainwright (“Happy Valley”) is miles away from the standard stuffy period drama, focusing, as it does, on the ways that its protagonist disrupted the conventions of her time. As played by Suranne Jones, Lister is charming and brilliant, dazzling others even when she’s not in the room, much to the consternation of her overlooked sister (Gemma Whelan). Scandalously good fun, this show.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Also arriving:
 </strong>
 “A Time to Kill” (April 1), “A Walk in the Clouds” (April 1), “Annie Hall” (April 1), “Hannah and Her Sisters” (April 1), “I Am Sam” (April 1), “Manhattan” (April 1), “Mean Girls” (April 1), “Repo Men” (April 1), “Splice” (April 1), “Suffragette” (April 1), “Veronica Mars” (April 1), “Waking Ned Devine” (April 1), “Crazy Rich Asians” (April 13), “BlacKkKlansman” (April 20), “2019 Rock &amp; Roll Hall of Fame Induction Ceremony” (April 27) and “On Tour with Asperger’s Are Us” Premiere (April 30).
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ‘
 </strong>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.hulu.com/series/ramy-4bcb6c3a-3d9a-4d49-b8e0-57fb7de9c8d6" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
   Ramy
  </strong>
 </a>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ’ Season 1
 </strong>
 <br/>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Starts streaming:
 </strong>
 April 19
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Ramy Hassan is going to die alone. Well, that’s what everybody keeps telling him. Ramy’s mother wants him to try picking up women at their local mosque in New Jersey, while one of his friends informs him that he’s running out of options (“There are only two hot Muslim girls left in town”). So naturally, Ramy is dating a Jewish woman. Ramy Youssef, the show’s star and creator, loosely based this half-hour comedy series on his own experiences as a first-generation Egyptian-American, and the humor comes from a very specific place. Balancing a spiritual life with a love life is both a serious issue and, as it turns out, a very funny one.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Also arriving:
 </strong>
 “The Addams Family” (April 1), “Akeelah and the Bee” (April 1), “Barton Fink” (April 1), “Blow” (April 1), “Cold Mountain” (April 1), “Days of Thunder” (April 1), “Eastern Promises” (April 1), “Everything Must Go” (April 1), “Happy Feet” (April 1), “In a World …” (April 1), “The Jerk” (April 1), “The Minus Man” (April 1), “Practical Magic” (April 1), “Primal Fear” (April 1), “The Seven Year Itch” (April 1), “The Shining” (April 1), “Six Degrees of Separation” (April 1), “Stories We Tell” (April 1), “Sunshine Cleaning” (April 1), “The Time Traveler’s Wife” (April 1), “Up in the Air” (April 1), “A Quiet Place” (April 2), “Destroyer” (April 9), “Enchanted April” (April 15) and “Waiting for ‘Superman’” (April 29).
</p><p class="css-1e7dx92 evys1bk0">
 An earlier version of this article misstated the given name of Lakeith Stanfield’s character in “Someone Great.” The character is Nate, not Nick.
</p><p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>

</p><p>
 Supported by
</p><p class="css-eqpmu6 e6idgb70">
 Endangered Spaces
</p>
<p class="css-h5kol3 e1wiw3jv0">
 Once a theater of staggering beauty, the long-shuttered RKO Keith’s in Flushing may soon be demolished.
</p><p class="g-address">
 135-35 Northern Boulevard, Queens
</p>
<p>
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2019/nyregion/endangered-spaces-nyc.html">
  Endangered Spaces
 </a>
 is celebrating beloved neighborhood institutions that are fighting to stay open in an era of relentless change. Tell us a space we should feature, and send us your memories, at
 <a href="mailto:endangeredspaces@nytimes.com">
  endangeredspaces@nytimes.com
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <a class="css-1riqqik e1jsehar0" href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/corey-kilgannon">
  <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
   Corey Kilgannon
  </span>
 </a>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 It never really mattered what movie was playing at the 3,000-seat RKO Keith’s Theater in Flushing, Queens. The main attraction was always the theater itself.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “It was enormous, a true movie palace,” recalled Jim Driscoll, 74, a lifelong Flushing resident. “They had a blue ceiling with special lights that looked like twinkling stars.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Wendy Antos-Baker, 70, who lives in Whitestone, Queens, said that “it had a very, very ornate, lobby — it was almost ostentatious, unlike any theater you’d see today.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Even though it closed in 1986 and has been deteriorating ever since, many devotees have fought to preserve the RKO Keith’s, even as it was flipped among developers seeking to demolish it and transform it into residential or commercial property.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The latest attempt — by Xinyuan Real Estate, a Beijing-based developer — seems to be underway and the RKO Keith’s days finally may be numbered.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “Every developer that came before us was playing games, looking to flip it, or slap up some building real fast,” said Eugene T. Kelty Jr., the chairman of the local community board. “Everyone took their profit and moved on. This is the only one who really wants to develop it.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The movie palace, Mr. Kelty said, was lovely, with an organ near the stage for pre-film entertainment.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 But, he added, its time has passed.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “It was a beautiful theater, I’ll give you that, but people want to go back in time and bring it back, which is a nice wish, but it’s just not happening,’’ Mr. Kelty said. “Too much of it is already destroyed.’’
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Still, Richard Thornhill, a
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.facebook.com/groups/220242754654213/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  local preservationist
 </a>
 , said that even in its decimated state, the theater can still be restored with help from photographs and by using the still-intact plans of its original designer, the renowned theater architect Thomas W. Lamb.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Mr. Thornhill has helped collect about 4,400 signatures
 <!-- -->
 on a petition to convince
 <!-- -->
 the developer to build around the theater and enable a renovation that would put Queens on footing with other boroughs that have restored historic theaters, including Kings Theater in Brooklyn, Paradise Theater in the Bronx and St. George Theatre on Staten Island.
</p>
<p class="css-1plcdrk evys1bk0">
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  “Everything in the Keith’s theater was beautiful — the candy stand in the lobby, even the bathrooms. They had an organ player, and they’d always run two movies, an A movie and then a B movie. It’s a shame that these big theaters are really all gone now.”
 </em>
</p>
<p class="css-1plcdrk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  —
 </strong>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Jim Driscoll, 74
 </strong>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The RKO Keith’s opened in 1928, and served partly as a vaudeville house for performers, including the Marx Brothers, Mae West, Bob Hope and Judy Garland.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Several generations saw their first film at the RKO Keith’s and attended local beauty contests and high school graduations there.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “We didn’t have television yet, so going to this magnificent movie house was a big deal,” said Claire K. Shulman, 93, who was the Queens borough president from 1986 until 2002. Her last visit was to see the 1979 sci-fi horror film “Alien” in a mezzanine where the air was thick with marijuana smoke.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 In its final years, the RKO Keith’s became a multiplex, and after it closed, much of the interior was gutted or damaged by vandalism, flooding and fires.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Boarded and bricked up, scarred with graffiti, it has languished as downtown Flushing has shifted from a largely white, middle-class neighborhood to one of the city’s largest Chinatowns.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 In 2016, Xinyuan bought the theater for $66 million with plans to turn it into a
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://ny.curbed.com/2017/5/16/15643646/rko-keiths-theater-landmark-redevelopment-queens" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  16-story modern glass tower,
 </a>
 with commercial space on lower floors and condominiums above.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 A Xinyuan spokesman said that plan would be modified “to meet the market demand as well as government requirements,” but gave no further details.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Xinyuan has gotten required approval from the city’s Landmarks Preservation Commission because the theater’s ticket lobby and foyer are protected as city landmarks.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Ms. Antos-Baker remembers studying the lobby one evening in 1977 after she
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.ranker.com/list/stories-from-people-who-don-t-like-star-wars/zack-howe" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  walked out
 </a>
 of “Star Wars” and waited for her friends who had remained inside watching the movie.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “I know most people loved ‘Star Wars,’’’ she said, “but I had a better time just looking at the lobby.”
</p><p class="css-1vs7yia interactive-leadin" data-testid="leadin" id="interactive-leadin">
 <b>
  NEW YORK CITY
 </b>
 is known around the world for the big stuff — iconic guidebook attractions like the Empire State Building and the Statue of Liberty. But New Yorkers know that the city’s essence lies in the smaller, neighborhood places that make up its unique fabric.
 <br/>
 <br/>
 If not well known, they are certainly well loved: the local dive bar or diner; the quirky Laundromat; the tiny shoe repair shop. And while the city is always in transition, the change these days seems relentless. Beloved spots are succumbing to high rents and are often replaced by chain businesses.
 <br/>
 <br/>
 Here then is a celebration of places that are closing (or just hanging on).
</p><p class="g-quote">
 “I wanted it to be a laboratory.”
</p>
<p class="g-quote">
 “These kind of comic shops are shutting down left and right.”
</p>
<p class="g-quote">
 “It’s been a comfort station for the neighborhood.”
</p>
<p class="g-quote">
 “Too much of it is already destroyed.’’
</p>
<p class="g-quote">
 “This is the Cheers of Park Slope.”
</p>
<p class="g-quote">
 “It’s been a true community effort.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ct2c9h interactive-credit" data-testid="credit" id="interactive-credit">
 Produced by Meghan Louttit, Eden Weingart, Gabriel Gianordoli and Diego Ribadeneira
 <br/>
 <br/>
 Julia Wertz is the author of Tenements, Towers and Trash: An Unconventional Illustrated History of New York City
</p><p class="css-1psfkbx etfikam0">
 Illustrations by Julia Wertz

  Character Study
 </a>
 column in the Sunday Metropolitan section. He was also part of the team that won the 2009 Pulitzer Prize for Breaking News.

 daily crossword column
</p>
<p class="css-z6dj7x e1wiw3jv0">
 Christopher Adams returns to scare the ever-loving you-know-what out of us.
</p>
<p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <a class="css-1riqqik e1jsehar0" href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/deb-amlen">
  <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
   Deb Amlen
  </span>
 </a>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/column/wordplay?module=inline" title="">
  THURSDAY PUZZLE
 </a>
 — A quick one today, as I am off to cover the 42nd
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.crosswordtournament.com/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  American Crossword Tournament
 </a>
 , which takes place March 22-24 in Stamford, Conn. If you are planning to attend, please come say “hi” and, if you can’t make it, I will be posting articles throughout the weekend to make you feel as if you are, in fact, there. And if you would like to solve the tournament puzzles,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.crosswordtournament.com/info/offer.htm" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  you can buy the set and solve them at home
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Christopher Adams, an independent crossword constructor with
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://arctanxwords.blogspot.com/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  his own site
 </a>
 , returns today with his second New York Times crossword, and I don’t know about you, but I think he’s trying to scare us.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 1A: Yes, The “Happening after the doors open on Black Friday” is technically a MAD DASH, but it’s really much worse than that, isn’t it? And it’s pretty much confined to the United States, although the concept of Black Friday has spread to other countries. Here, for example, is the difference between the beginning of a Black Friday shopping experience in Canada and one in the United States, where the first fight breaks out on the left side of your screen at about 1:10:
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 16A: Bonus points if you knew how to spell Ms. Ronan’s name without Googling it. Even though I’m a fan and have some inkling of how to spell her beautiful Irish name — SAOIRSE — I had to look it up.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 49D: Isn’t butterscotch more of a tan/brown color? I have never thought of it as ORANGE, but maybe I just see it differently.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Before we discuss the theme, I’m told that Across Lite might not be able to handle the entry of this theme if you use the intended symbol, but it will accept the answer if you type out the word or the first letter of that word.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Mr. Adams offers us a set of three films that have right slashes in their titles, such as “FROST/NIXON,” and their crossers (while not films) have slashes as well. All of this is neatly tied up by the revealer at 25D, SLASHER FILM, which is normally a phrase that describes a movie with a lot of carnage in it.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 As Mr. Adams notes below, I was a bit concerned by the entry 7D, HE/SHE and its cluing, but I was excited that there might be a possibility that the crossers for these film titles related back to them. For example, the theme entry at 17A is “VICTOR/VICTORIA,” which, on its surface, is about Julie Andrews’s character cross-dressing to get a job, but is really a look at the spectrum of queerness.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Alas, they’re not all related, but they all work on their own.
</p>
<p class="css-1plcdrk evys1bk0">
 This is my second New York Times puzzle, and it was made about the same time my
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.xwordinfo.com/Crossword?date=7/3/2018" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  first
 </a>
 one was published. And like that puzzle, this one uses left/right symmetry because of the lengths of the theme answers. But as an
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://arctanxwords.blogspot.com/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  indie constructor
 </a>
 , I enjoy doing unconventional things to get themes to work, and I’m always pleased when they do.
</p>
<p class="css-1plcdrk evys1bk0">
 The hardest part of this one, by far, was fitting in the FACE/OFF entry. Because it’s an even-length answer, I couldn’t run it horizontally, so it had to go in the center column. And the left/right symmetry meant having two answers of (at minimum) eight letters flank it and intersect with whatever entry went through the slash. It took a lot of trial and error to get it to work, but I’m pleased with the result. That said, the shape of the center largely forced the rest of the grid, including making 7D six spaces long. (More on this in a bit.) I could have broken up the top two rows into three answers each, but I try to avoid too many shorter answers when possible, and I’m pleased with how these corners came out.
</p>
<p class="css-1plcdrk evys1bk0">
 And yet, I am a bit conflicted about 7D, HE/SHE. As clued, it’s a perfectly reasonable answer in that it is unquestionably a third-person pronoun. Many people will see no problem with this clue/answer combo and will move on without a second thought. But others won’t. I, for one, had some reservations about it then, and I don’t think I’d use this answer if I were making this puzzle today. When the puzzle was accepted, Will and his team agreed with me that this was a clue to handle delicately. After all, these pronouns are restrictive and needlessly exclude nonbinary people, and have largely been supplanted by the singular “they” anyway. There was a fair bit of discussion about how to address such concerns without writing a clue that was awkwardly worded or overly wordy, or that called too much attention to the answer. In the end, we agreed that, as written, the clue would work best for the previously mentioned reason: It works without distracting from the rest of the puzzle. Still, even though this issue is not addressed in the clue, I’m glad Will and his team took the time to discuss it carefully and to consider multiple viewpoints and suggestions.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Almost finished solving but need a
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  bit
 </em>
 more help? We’ve got you covered.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Warning: There be spoilers ahead, but
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/svc/crosswords/v2/puzzle/16455.ans.pdf?module=inline" title="">
  subscribers can take a peek at the answer key
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Trying to get back to the puzzle page?
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/crosswords?module=inline" title="">
  Right here
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Your thoughts?
</p><p>
 Deb Amlen is the crossword columnist. She has been writing the
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/column/wordplay">
  Wordplay column
 </a>
 since 2011 and believes that everyone can learn to solve the Times crossword. Even the Saturdays.

    @
   </span>
   NYTimesWordplay
  </a>

 Supported by
</p><p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
  Reuters
 </span>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 LOS ANGELES — The directors of "Avengers: Endgame" pleaded with fans on Tuesday not to spoil the movie by giving away storylines after reports that some scenes had leaked online.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 In an open letter posted on Twitter under the hashtag #DontSpoilTheEndgame, Joe and Anthony Russo said they and the vast cast of the upcoming Marvel superhero movie "have worked tirelessly for the last three years with the sole intention of delivering a surprising and emotionally powerful conclusion" to the saga.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 "When you see Endgame in coming weeks, please don't spoil it for others, the same way you wouldn't want it spoiled for you," they added.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Walt Disney Co's "Avengers: Endgame" marks the conclusion of a story told across 22 Marvel films. The plot has been shrouded in secrecy, with no advance screenings for the entertainment press. Sales of advance tickets earlier this month surpassed those of 2015 movie "Star Wars: The Force Awakens."
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The film, which brings together multiple comic book characters, starts its rollout on April 24 in Australia and China before arriving in the United States on April 25.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Some fans said on Tuesday they had seen brief, grainy scenes on Reddit, YouTube and other platforms, but the footage was swiftly removed. Reuters has not seen the leaked scenes and Disney declined to comment.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The #DontSpoilTheEnding hashtag was one of the top Twitter trends on Tuesday. Some fans who said they had viewed the leaks said they only increased their anticipation for the movie.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 "I was upset for about 0.2 seconds then realized how cool it was and it made me so hyped," a person with the user name thestaggie posted on Reddit.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Chris Smith, a contributor to the BGR.com entertainment and tech news site, wrote that he had seen a leak but that it "doesn't really give away the ending" of the movie, although it contained scenes that had not been shown in any of the trailers or official clips released so far.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 "I don’t consider that the leak has actually ruined the movie for me. It just makes me want to watch it even more than I already did," wrote Smith.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 (Reporting by Jill Serjeant; Editing by Peter Cooney)
