</p><p>
 Supported by
</p><p class="css-z6dj7x e1wiw3jv0">
 MoviePass made waves when it launched, but it’s not the only movie subscription designed to lure you to the theater. Here are a few popular ones, who they’re for and whether you’ll save money with one.
</p>
<p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
  Eric Ravenscraft
 </span>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 With the summer blockbuster season starting earlier than ever, an all-you-can-eat theater subscription sounds appealing. Then again so do gym memberships and
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/10/17/smarter-living/clothing-box-services-stitch-fix-trunk-club.html?module=inline" title="">
  subscription boxes
 </a>
 . Likewise, whether they’re worth it depends on how you use them.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.moviepass.com/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  MoviePass
 </a>
 made waves in 2017 when it introduced a radical — if unsustainable — plan. For $10 a month, you could watch a movie every day at any theater you want. You could even cancel any time you want. It sounded like the perfect deal.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Unfortunately, it was too good to be true. The company paid the same ticket price you would for every ticket, which meant it almost always lost money the first time you went to the theater. This didn’t lend itself to a sustainable business model. Since 2017, MoviePass burned through millions of dollars paying for movie tickets,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/07/29/business/moviepass-service-outage-finances.html?module=inline" title="">
  briefly shut down a couple times
 </a>
 when it couldn’t pay its bills and was eventually forced to dramatically water down its service just to stay afloat.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The stock price for Helios and Matheson, MoviePass’s parent company, has plummeted and was recently
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.marketwatch.com/story/moviepass-parent-helios-mathesons-stock-to-be-delisted-at-the-open-and-the-company-wont-fight-it-2019-02-13" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  delisted from the Nasdaq
 </a>
 . The $7.25 million worth of stock that MoviePass’s own CEO had in 2017 is
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://qz.com/1491219/the-ceo-of-moviepasss-parent-company-lost-millions-to-his-companys-diving-stock/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  worth about $50 today
 </a>
 . Not $50 million. $50. Not enough to buy six months of MoviePass.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Despite the disaster of MoviePass’s specific business model, imitators have popped up.
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.sinemia.com/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Sinemia
 </a>
 offers a similar plan, but with much more limitations (and thus more sustainability), AMC launched its own subscription
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.google.com/search?q=amc+alist&amp;oq=amc+alist&amp;aqs=chrome.0.0l6.1996j0j7&amp;sourceid=chrome&amp;ie=UTF-8" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  called A-List
 </a>
 that only works within its own theater chain and MoviePass even revamped its own plans to cut its losses.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Arguably, MoviePass’s first mistake with its ill-fated subscription was having few terms to limit its use. It appealed to moviegoers, but was bound to be problematic business-wise: You could watch any movie at any theater any time, all for less than the price of a single ticket at most theaters. Today’s subscriptions are a little more complicated (or expensive), with arcane rules that change their value proposition entirely. With that in mind, let’s go over what each one costs, the limitations of each and who they’re best suited for.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 MoviePass plans change almost too frequently to keep up with. During the process of writing and editing this article, the company overhauled its plans entirely, only three months after
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/07/31/movies/moviepass-timeline.html?module=inline" title="">
  the last time it overhauled its plans entirely
 </a>
 . In another six months it may change again. For now, you have two options with MoviePass:
</p>
<p class="css-1e7dx92 evys1bk0">
 For $19.95 a month, you can watch up to one 2D movie a day at most major theater chains including AMC, Regal and Cinemark. For a limited time after its introduction, users could sign up for $14.95 a month, but the company gave no indication how long this deal would last or how long that price would be locked in for.
</p><p class="css-1e7dx92 evys1bk0">
 Users can lock in a $9.95 per month rate by paying for the entire year up front. This gets you the same 2D movie per day as the monthly plan, but you save about $120 every year. It should be noted that since first introducing its original
 <!-- -->
 $10 per month
 <!-- -->
 pla
 <!-- -->
 n
 <!-- -->
 in 2017, MoviePass hasn’t gone a straight 12 months without changing its plan, and even users who pay by the year have been affected by changes. The Terms of Service specifically note that MoviePass has the right to change its service at any time.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 These plans come with some serious caveats, though. For starters, you can only sign up for that annual plan price if you hand over your bank account information. Monthly plan users can sign up with a debit or credit card. In the past, MoviePass has made questionable charges that users (or former users)
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://lifehacker.com/make-sure-moviepass-didnt-reactivate-your-subscription-1829491233" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  have had to dispute
 </a>
 . Disputing charges made with your bank account information is possible, but
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.quora.com/What-procedures-are-typically-required-to-reverse-or-repudiate-an-ACH-transaction" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  more difficult
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 In addition to the above, the plan comes with many restrictions: You can’t watch a movie more than once. If you already used MoviePass to watch the latest Spider-Man movie, you’re going to have to find some other superhero movie to watch next time or pay out of pocket. If you watch too many movies, you might also be cut off. According to the Terms of Service:
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  This means that MoviePass has the right to limit the selection of movies and/or the times of available movies should your individual use adversely impact MoviePass’s system-wide capacity or the availability of the Service for other subscribers.
 </em>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Additionally, for most theater chains — including the big ones like AMC, Regal and Cinemark — you can’t reserve seats or order online. Instead, you have to be physically at the theater in order to buy a ticket, and you can only buy them on the day you plan to watch the movie. This makes it much harder to plan a movie night ahead of time.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Right off the bat, you have to be a bit of a risk taker to use MoviePass. Its plan sounds remarkably similar to the plan that nearly shut it down in the first place, but there’s no telling when the company will change its plans, or if it will even still be in business a year from now. And paying for the annual plan via bank transfer is especially risky, as it makes it harder to dispute charges and you have to trust MoviePass to secure your banking info properly.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 If you can get over those many hurdles, MoviePass is ideal for users who want to have their choice of theater, and who will see at least two to three movies per month. The
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.boxofficemojo.com/about/adjuster.htm" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  average ticket price in the US
 </a>
 in 2018 was $9.14, which is less than even the cheapest version of the MoviePass plan. Depending on your region, MoviePass can be up to $20 for just 2D showings. You’ll rarely break even on just one movie per month, but two would be a start. If you can hit your limit of all three movies, that would be even better. For the cheapest version, you’ll need to see two movies per month, and the most expensive option would need three or more. Of course, the more you watch, the more likely you are to hit that invisible wall that could start to limit your selection.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 By comparison, AMC’s theater subscription plan is much more generous, but slightly more expensive than MoviePass. There’s only one plan, which costs between $19.95 to $23.95 depending on where you live. According to AMC, pricing is $19.95 in 35 states, and only markets where ticket prices are higher than average cost more.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 With the A-List plan, you get the following benefits:
</p>
<p class="css-1e7dx92 evys1bk0">
 You can watch up to three movies per week. There are no restrictions on which specific movies you can watch, though A-List can’t be used on special shows like Fathom Events.
</p><p class="css-1e7dx92 evys1bk0">
 You can choose 2D, 3D, IMAX or Dolby showings for any of your movies. There’s no upcharge for “premium” showings.
</p><p class="css-1e7dx92 evys1bk0">
 You can order ahead and even reserve seats at theaters with assigned seating. There’s no time limit on how far ahead you can order a ticket, though there are limitations on how many reservations you can have at once.
</p><p class="css-1e7dx92 evys1bk0">
 Since A-List is part of the Stubs program, you also get discounts and $5 rewards for every $50 spent. The cost of A-List counts toward that reward, which means a
 <!-- -->
 $20 per mont
 <!-- -->
 h
 <!-- -->
 subscription comes out closer to
 <!-- -->
 $18 per mont
 <!-- -->
 h
 <!-- -->
 .
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 AMC could hypothetically offer up to 156 showings per year. While MoviePass claims you can watch a movie a day, it also offers vague threats to limit users who use it “excessively.” It’s also roughly the same price as MoviePass’s monthly plan, but that includes 3D and IMAX showings. Unfortunately, AMC doesn’t offer a lower price tier if you only care about 2D movies.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 While you potentially get more movies, there’s one peculiar limitation. You can only have three active “reservations” at a time. While you’re allowed to watch three movies per week, you need to have at least one reservation slot open in order to buy a ticket. If you pre-buy tickets for three movies next month, you can’t order an A-List ticket this week unless you cancel one of those reservations. This usually only affects very heavy movie goers, but it can be an inconvenient hurdle if you like to plan ahead.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 A-List is for AMC lovers, first and foremost. If you prefer Regal or Cinemark (or don’t live near an AMC theater), then you’re out. On the other hand, if you can handle the chain lock-in, then A-List can be worth it for as little as one movie a month. IMAX, 3D, and Dolby showings are included — and those tickets can get expensive. In some rare cases, a single premium showing can pay for A-List in a month.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 More likely, you’ll need to see at least two or more movies a month to get your money back, and the more the better. Heavy users will be able to get far more than their money’s worth out of it compared to other plans. It’s not unlimited, but it’s the closest thing to it on the market right now.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Sinemia has been a quiet competitor to MoviePass for years, but with a much more sustainable (if less appealing) business model. The company offers more options than we have the space to go through, so we’ll focus on just a few.
</p>
<p class="css-1e7dx92 evys1bk0">
 For $7.99 a month (billed annually), you can watch up to three 2D movies every month. There’s no limit on which movies you can watch, and you can even order advance tickets, though the process to do so is complicated. If you don’t use all three of your movies, one can rollover to the next month.
</p><p class="css-1e7dx92 evys1bk0">
 For the $8.99 a month (also billed annually) Plus plan, you can watch up to two movies per month, including 3D and IMAX showings.
</p><p class="css-1e7dx92 evys1bk0">
 For $14.99 a month, you can watch up to 30 2D movies per month. Unlike AMC’s A-List, there’s no weekly limit, so if you want to spend every single day in the theater, you can.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The unlimited monthly plan comes with a $30 initiation fee, which means it might take a while before you break even. The other plans must be billed annually, which means you’re taking a risk that you’ll get the most out of your plans before the year is done.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 If none of those plans fit your needs, Sinemia offers a few more plans in between with various specific details, including only one or two movies a month. The company is also the only one we found that
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.sinemia.com/for-two/new-plans/us/los-angeles?plan_type=3" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  offers family plans
 </a>
 that scale up to six people.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 On top of the monthly plans, Sinemia has recently added a $1.80 “processing fee” per movie. This fee has become the subject of
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.businessinsider.com/class-action-lawsuit-against-moviepass-competitor-sinemia-heating-up-2019-3" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  a class-action lawsuit from existing users
 </a>
 . Additionally, some users have complained that their accounts have been canceled without warning. While Sinemia claims this is due to fraudulent activity, many users insist that this is not the case.
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.bbb.org/us/ca/west-hollywood/profile/online-retailer/sinemia-inc-1216-883526/complaints" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  An influx of reviews on the Better Business Bureau
 </a>
 have also cited app crashes and unresponsive customer support.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Sinemia used to be more reliable but it’s recently had the same woes as MoviePass. There’s no real quota you’d have to hit to make Sinemia worth it because if you don’t use it enough, you can just drop to a lower tier. One movie a month costs $4. Three movies costs $8. Every plan costs less than the national average for movie ticket prices, so long as you use it.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 However, Sinemia’s annual billing and “membership initiation fee” throws a wrench in the gears. If you pay by the month for the unlimited plan, Sinemia will charge you at least $30 to sign up, which can eat into those savings. You’d have to see almost five movies to break even in the first month. For the cheaper plans, it’s easier to break even, but only if you use the plan throughout the year.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 It’s also hard to overlook the recent warning signs from Sinemia. While the company has operated with minimal controversy for years prior, its recent policy of new fees and closing accounts might make it almost as risky as MoviePass. However, if you like to dive into the details of a plan and make absolutely sure you’re getting the best bang for your buck, Sinemia is probably for you.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  This article has been updated with information regarding a class-action lawsuit against Sinemia.
 </em>
</p><p>

 Advertisement
</p><p>
 Supported by
</p><p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <a class="css-1riqqik e1jsehar0" href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/brooks-barnes">
  <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
   Brooks Barnes
  </span>
 </a>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 WEST HOLLYWOOD, Calif. — Fred Rosen, the retired Ticketmaster tycoon, was eating a melted ham-and-brie sandwich at the exclusive
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.sanvicentebungalows.com/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  San Vicente Bungalows
 </a>
 and spouting forth about belts.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Yes, what people use to hold up their pants. You can buy one at Walmart for $4, he noted. Or you can get one at Gucci for $1,500. “Every product I can think of has a luxury version, which got me thinking,” Mr. Rosen said. “Why not movies?”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 It’s an idea that has captivated one entrepreneur after another over the years: For a high price, allow tech billionaires, Wall Street titans, professional athletes, Russian oligarchs and other ultra-wealthy people to rent movies — as soon as they come out in theaters — for viewing at home. Think of it like Netflix for one-percenters. But such upstarts have always sputtered, including one backed by Best Buy in 2013 that charged
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.latimes.com/entertainment/envelope/la-xpm-2013-apr-09-la-fi-ct-rich-people-cinema-20130409-story.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  $500 per movie
 </a>
 on top of $35,000 in setup costs. Film studios, fearful of angering theater chains, have been reluctant to participate. Piracy has also been a concern.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Mr. Rosen, 75, and a septuagenarian golfing buddy, Dan Fellman, who is Hollywood’s
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2015/07/30/business/media/dan-fellman-executive-at-warner-bros-to-leave-studio.html?module=inline" title="">
  foremost film distribution expert
 </a>
 , may have finally figured out how to make it work. They have quietly founded
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.redcarpethomecinema.com/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Red Carpet Home Cinema
 </a>
 , which rents first-run films for $1,500 to $3,000 each. Red Carpet has contracts with Warner Bros., Paramount, Lionsgate, Annapurna and Disney’s 20th Century Fox and Fox Searchlight divisions — resulting in some 40 movies annually, including blockbusters like “Aquaman” and “A Star Is Born.”
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Those partnerships reflect entertainment-industry relationships that Mr. Rosen and Mr. Fellman have cultivated over decades. Most studios do not see them as disrupters from Silicon Valley, something that has
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://variety.com/2016/film/news/warner-bros-kevin-tsujihara-screening-room-1201752023/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  stalled start-ups
 </a>
 like
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.latimes.com/entertainment/envelope/cotown/la-et-ct-sean-parker-20160405-story.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Screening Room
 </a>
 , which has tried without success since 2016 to speed first-run movies to homes for a premium price. (Sean Parker of Napster and Facebook fame is behind that one.)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Red Carpet also arrives at a time when the movie industry is undergoing sweeping change — not the least of which involves the manner in which Netflix is
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/16/business/media/netflix-movies-hollywood.html?module=inline" title="">
  challenging the traditional way that films are released
 </a>
 . For the most part, theater owners insist on a three-month period of exclusivity to play new films. Netflix has started to chip away at that practice, offering theaters an exclusive window of three weeks or less for films like “Roma” and “Bird Box.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Most studios see broader distribution change as inevitable, noted Harold L. Vogel, author of the textbook “Entertainment Industry Economics.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “Consumers want to have more control,” Mr. Vogel said.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Even so, studios are treading carefully. None of the film companies that have signed on as Red Carpet partners would discuss the venture publicly. Several major movie operations, including Universal, Sony Pictures and Disney’s other labels, are Red Carpet holdouts. They also declined to comment.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “I feel pretty comfortable that we can gain more studio partners,” Mr. Rosen said. “We are a niche offering — I’m too old for disruption — but even if a studio makes $25 million to $50 million annually from us, that’s found money.”
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Theater owners, in the meantime, seem to be taking a wait-and-see approach to Red Carpet.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “I have no take on that,” said Adam Aron, chief executive of AMC Entertainment, the dominant theater chain in the United States. In contrast, Mr. Aron has readily criticized start-ups like MoviePass, the
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/05/business/media/moviepass-subscription-plan.html?module=inline" title="">
  subscription ticketing service
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The folksy Mr. Rosen, who took over Ticketmaster in 1982 and helped
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2011/06/12/business/12tickets.html?module=inline" title="">
  turn it into a goliath
 </a>
 , and Mr. Fellman, who started his studio career in 1964, worked out details for Red Carpet over rounds of golf at the well-to-do Hillcrest Country Club in Los Angeles. The luxury service operates a bit like a private club. There is a rigorous application process, and participants must have a credit card with a limit of at least $50,000. Those who become customers must buy a $15,000 box that connects to a home theater system (installed by a technician) and comes loaded with piracy protections.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Prices for rentals are set by the participating studios, with higher fees for blockbuster-style movies like “Shazam!” and lower costs for dramas like “The Shape of Water.” Each rental allows for two viewings in a 36-hour period.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 How big could Red Carpet get? There are more people who can afford it than you might think. Nearly 46,000 Americans have annual income of
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.ssa.gov/cgi-bin/netcomp.cgi?year=2017" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  more than $2 million
 </a>
 , according to Social Security Administration data from 2017. Mr. Rosen and Mr. Fellman, however, insisted over lunch in mid-March that they were not interested in size.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “We’re not even looking for 10,000 people,” Mr. Rosen said. With fewer than 4,000 customers, Red Carpet could have $300 million in annual revenue, according to Mr. Fellman’s projections.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Red Carpet, which counts Sherry Lansing, the former chief executive of Paramount, as an investor, has been operating in about 25 homes as part of a beta test since December. “I’m recommending the service to my friends,” the Red Carpet website quotes Ms. Lansing as saying.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Mr. Fellman added: “I’m not interested in starting a business that is disruptive to the theatrical experience. Maybe we get 400 homes in New York and L.A. Maybe 100 in each of the 30 biggest cities in the United States.” (Celebrities and Hollywood big shots have long been able to see first-run films in their homes — free — as part of something known as the Bel-Air Circuit. Studios allow a limited number of
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.wsj.com/articles/SB10001424052970203986604577253852745599184" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  preapproved V.I.P.s
 </a>
 to “borrow” copies of new movies.)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “We told studios, ‘You set the terms,’” Mr. Fellman said. “They appreciated that. What doesn’t work in Hollywood is going in and wagging a finger and saying, ‘This is how it’s going to be.’”
</p><p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>
<p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>
<p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>

 Supported by
</p><p class="css-z6dj7x e1wiw3jv0">
 The sublime liberation of caring about being funny most of all.
</p>
<p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <a class="css-1riqqik e1jsehar0" href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/maeve-higgins">
  <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
   Maeve Higgins
  </span>
 </a>
</p>
<p class="css-ri4qrz e1wtpvyy1">
 Contributing Opinion Writer
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 I don’t know if you’re the type of person who can’t wait to get out of your clothes as soon as you get in the door from work, but I am.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 I love to peel off too-tight trousers and wriggle free of restrictive blouses and, best of all, escape the double clutch of a bra. Feeling free and looking insane, I wander half-dressed through my apartment on my phone, hunting for snacks. This is the least self-conscious moment of the day, a private ritual that would look funny to anyone watching. Usually, nobody is. Once, though, somebody was. An entire film crew, actually.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 And several people were taping, so now I suppose anyone who watches the film will see me struggle out of a woolen skirt, letting it fall to the ground in an unsexy heap. Then they will watch me, in tan-colored control-top pantyhose and a chunky knit turtleneck, lean into the refrigerator looking for one of those yogurts with the candy corners before sitting heavily on an exercise ball and devouring the yogurt as I flick through a gossip magazine.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The film premiered last week, and I got to see it for the first time. Lots of firsts for me that day: first time being in a movie, first lead role, first time seeing my butt up there on the big screen at the same time 300 strangers did.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 That last part was my favorite, which is surprising to me. I’ve never really liked how I look. Please don’t correct me or get mad at me when I tell you that. I appreciate that I’m able-bodied and I get to move around the world with that privilege. I am in awe of the body-positivity movement, but despite huge efforts toward it, when I look in the mirror I’m either ambivalent or full-on
 <!-- -->
 Harvey Pekar
 <!-- -->
 muttering, “Well, there’s a reliable disappointment.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 I understand there are a hundred reasons for this — societal, systemic, psychological and so on. But I no longer devote energy to figuring them out. I have finite time and resources, so I get on with more important things like understanding the Green New Deal or really getting to know my Instant Pot or, best of all, doing anything and everything I can think of to be funny.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Funniness to me is simultaneously an escape hatch and a way of facing reality. In dark times and in sad places I can still usually find it, at least enough of it to keep me going.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 One muffled fart expertly placed, by me, close to a nun and in front of a class got me through my personal annus horribilis, my 14th year. I traveled to Erbil, Iraq, in 2016 for a comedy workshop, when the Islamic State still controlled a big chunk of the
 <!-- -->
 country
 <!-- -->
 . My peers there taught me that jokes can and must be deployed by those who need them most. I still laugh when I remember a stand-up comic there and a joke he told, in perfectly poor taste, about an unsatisfied wife. We follow each other on Instagram, and the year after we met I watched as he donned his pesh merga uniform and joined the battle to take back Mosul.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The older I get, the less sense the world makes, but thankfully, funny is a renewable resource.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 I imagine that because I’ve nurtured this resource, I got to make this film. I’m still surprised it exists. It has been seven years since my friends, and now my directors, Mike and Enda, first mumbled the idea to me in a Dublin pub. “Wouldn’t it be a gas if you were kind of a lonely driving instructor and you could talk to ghosts?”
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 And now this very silly supernatural romantic comedy exists. I thought about my friends, who wrote and directed this lonely ghost-talking driving instructor movie, when I watched the comedian Chelsea Peretti
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=4qiMQWYOa0c" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  host
 </a>
 the Writers Guild Awards last month. “Every woman is obsessed with procuring a flipped role — a role that’s good that’s written for a guy and then they just name it Esmerelda”
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.latimes.com/entertainment/movies/la-et-mn-writers-guild-awards-winners-20190217-story.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  she
 </a>
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.latimes.com/entertainment/movies/la-et-mn-writers-guild-awards-winners-20190217-story.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  said
 </a>
 . Why not just write a good role for a woman, she asked the roomful of nervously grinning writers in formal wear. “Is that too kooky? Here’s a simple way to do it if you’re struggling — you know how you like guys? Just imagine you liked women.” The laughter that followed had a wince to it, because of the truth in the joke.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 I’m not claiming to be the first to deliberately set aside self-consciousness in order to focus on the funny. Many women have cast off expectations and inhibitions and gone straight for the laughs.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “Muriel’s Wedding,” starring a gauche and hilarious Toni Collette, is my gold standard. I’m a Natasha Rothwell fangirl, and while she is a hugely successful creator and writer with a strong political voice in Hollywood, she also plays Kelli on
 <!-- -->
 HBO’s
 <!-- -->
 “Insecure,” the one who makes me laugh out loud with her buoyant silliness and generosity. In Tina Fey’s memoir, “Bossypants,” Ms. Fey
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://slate.com/technology/2011/07/tina-fey-s-bossypants-an-excerpt.html" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  recalls a time
 </a>
 when Amy Poehler, new to “Saturday Night Live,” was doing a goofy bit in the writers’ room. Jimmy Fallon interrupted her and said: “Stop that! It’s not cute! I don’t like it.” Ms. Fey describes what happened next: “Amy dropped what she was doing, went black in the eyes for a second, and wheeled around on him.” She told him, forcefully, that she didn’t care whether he liked it.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 But enough about them; back to me. I’ve been feeling very important because I’m in a movie. Earlier this year, I emailed my agent to ask something:
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  I’m the lead, so I’ll be on the poster, right?
 </em>
 He said he’d check, and then the email trail went cold, which always means the answer is not the one I want. Weeks later, I saw the poster. I’m not on it — there’s just a ghost peeking out of a garbage bin. I laughed a lot when I saw that.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 I take myself too seriously, probably because I’m a human being. With our big brains and our bigger egos, we can’t help building the case to ourselves and others that we are very important creatures, when really we’re ludicrous. To be funny is to remind ourselves again and again just how ridiculous we are.
</p>
<p class="css-1psfkbx etfikam0">
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="http://www.maevehiggins.com/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Maeve Higgins
 </a>
 is the author of “Maeve in America: Essays by a Girl From Somewhere Else” and a contributing opinion writer.
</p>

</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The
 <!-- -->
 chatter in certain New York society circles these days isn’t so much about who’s been invited, as who’s been indicted.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Last Friday, just after Michael D. Cohen, President Trump’s
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/12/12/nyregion/michael-cohen-sentence-trump.html?module=inline" title="">
  prison-bound
 </a>
 former lawyer,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/02/27/us/politics/cohen-testimony.html?module=inline" title="">
  testified before
 </a>
 Congress, several dozen socialites gathered at the United Nations for an awards luncheon celebrating the
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.unwomenforpeace.org/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  UN Women for Peace Association
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 It was hard to miss
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/01/18/fashion/inauguration-donald-trump-stephanie-winston-wolkoff.html?module=inline">
  <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2017/01/18/fashion/inauguration-donald-trump-stephanie-winston-wolkoff.html" title="">
   Stephanie Winston Wolkoff
  </a>
 </a>
 , an association board member, whose 6-foot-1-inch frame was boosted by two-inch Manolo Blahnik heels. A close associate of Mr. Cohen, she had
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/02/04/us/politics/trump-inaugural-committee-subpoena.html?module=inline" title="">
  helped organize the president’s $107 million inauguration
 </a>
 , the financing of which is now being investigated by federal prosecutors.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “Alexander McQueen, head to toe,” Ms. Winston Wolkoff said of her black suit with red accents.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Very chic. Also, have you been subpoenaed by the feds?
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “I am not going to comment whether I am part of the investigation or not,” she said, without missing a beat.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">

 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">

  </a>

 , the filmmaker;

   Ben Stiller
  </a>
 </a>
 , the actor;
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://india.blogs.nytimes.com/2011/10/03/a-conversation-with-naeem-khan/" title="">

  </a>

 , the fashion designer;

 and

 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/05/05/sports/albert-pujols-3000th-hit-.html?module=inline">

  </a>
 </a>
 , a baseball player, and his wife,
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Deidre
 </strong>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Guests included
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2014/01/16/fashion/Karolina-Kurkova-Czech-fashion-model.html?ref=fashion&amp;module=inline">

  </a>
 </strong>
 , the wife of the Kuwaiti ambassador;
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2012/11/29/fashion/saving-the-endangered-dinner-party.html?module=inline">

 </strong>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The luncheon was pegged to International Women’s Day on March 8 and raised around $500,000 for United Nations programs benefiting at-risk women. But the politesse of the occasion was lost on one man, who bowled over to discuss Ms. Hurd’s role as a villain on “
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/column/blindspot-tv-recaps?module=inline" title="">
  Blindspot

 .”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “I love your work,” he said, clasping her with both his hands. “You’re the ultimate bitch.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Perhaps more on-message for International Women’s Day is “Gloria Bell,” a film about a woman dealing with the travails of midlife, starring
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Julianne Moore

 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  John Turturro

 .

 Nonetheless, Ms. Moore made it clear that she doesn’t have room in her calendar for all those extra commemorative holidays.

<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “I hate those kinds of celebratory days,” she said at a screening of the film on Monday night at the Museum of Modern Art. “Like, I hate Mother’s Day. I just hate it. It’s a manufactured holiday. I’m always, like, ‘I don’t want to have brunch. I just want to spend time with my family.’”

<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Her remarks were interrupted by several cast members of the “The Real Housewives of New York,” who arrived screeching like a flock of parrots.

<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/02/23/style/real-housewives-luann-de-lesseps-arrest-cabaret.html?module=inline">

<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 They were given a wide berth from the other guests, including
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/2014/01/19/movies/sebastian-lelios-gloria-flirts-with-metaphor.html?module=inline">

  </a>
 </a>
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  ,

 the film’s writer and director;
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Julian Schnabel
 </strong>
 , the artist, in paint-splattered boots;
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">

 , the celebrated soprano; and

<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Ms. Moore, who wore a sleeveless Givenchy dress made of ostrich feathers, returned to the topic at hand.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “I hope you feel inspired to live when you watch this movie,” she said, smiling warmly as staff members worked to right the signage on the red carpet.

</p><p>
 Supported by
</p><p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
  The Associated Press
 </span>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 NEW YORK — Woody Allen breached his four-movie deal with the online giant by making statements about the #MeToo movement that damaged prospects for promoting his films, an Amazon lawyer said Friday.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Attorney Robert Klieger told U.S. District Judge Denise Cote that the company protected itself after Allen made "public comments that at a minimum were insensitive to the #MeToo movement."
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The hearing was related to a lawsuit Allen filed in February seeking at least $68 million in damages. The lawsuit said Amazon ended his 2017 contract in June without ever releasing a completed film, "A Rainy Day in New York."
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Allen was not in court. John Quinn, his lawyer, told the judge that Seattle-based Amazon initially claimed it was ending the deal because of allegations made against Allen, not because of his recent statements.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 "The baseless allegations against Mr. Allen are decades old," Quinn said. He added that the claims were well known when Amazon signed its deal with Allen in August 2017.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Allen's adopted daughter, Dylan Farrow, said in 1992 that Allen molested her in an attic when she was 7. Allen has repeatedly denied it.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 In 2014, Farrow wrote about the allegations and then appeared in an interview early last year for the first time.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Quinn said Amazon has since changed its explanation for terminating the contract to blaming Allen for making statements saying that the #MeToo movement should not become a witch hunt.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The lawyer said the company had also cited claims that people in the industry won't work with Allen.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Regardless, Quinn said, Allen planned to produce a new movie this summer.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 In court papers, Amazon noted that Allen's son, Ronan Farrow, published an article in The New Yorker just weeks after the company signed its contract with Allen. The article detailed reports of sexual misconduct against movie producer Harvey Weinstein. Weinstein has denied non-consensual sexual conduct occurred.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 "The article became the catalyst for a broad reckoning over the persistence of sexual harassment in entertainment and other industries," Amazon's lawyers wrote.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 They said Allen then made a series of public comments "suggesting that he failed to grasp the gravity of the issues or the implications for his own career."
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 They cited several quotes by Allen in a magazine article including: "You don't want it to lead to a witch-hunt atmosphere, a Salem atmosphere, where every guy in an office who winks at a woman is suddenly having to call a lawyer to defend himself."
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 They also noted that after Dylan Farrow recounted her memories of sexual abuse in January 2018, Allen publicly dismissed the statements in a newspaper article as "cynically using" #MeToo for attention.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Klieger told the judge Allen's public comments resulted in controversy "in Hollywood and outside of Hollywood."
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The effect of the comments mean the pictures can no longer be made or promoted, Klieger said.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 After hearing both lawyers, the judge set a case schedule stretching into next year.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Allen and possibly Amazon founder Jeff Bezos, among others, were likely to provide depositions unless the case is resolved through private mediation in coming weeks.
</p><p>

</p><p class="css-4oyyp6">
 When you purchase a ticket for an independently reviewed film through our site, we earn an affiliate commission.
</p><p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <a class="css-1riqqik e1jsehar0" href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/ben-kenigsberg">
  <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
   Ben Kenigsberg
  </span>
 </a>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Like “United 93,” “Hotel Mumbai” begins from the uncomfortable premise of turning an actual terrorist incident into material for a dramatized suspense feature. In November 2008, 10 men
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2008/11/27/world/asia/27mumbai.html?module=inline" title="">
  unleashed gunfire and grenade assaults across Mumbai
 </a>
 , killing more than 160 people.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The film, inspired by a documentary, “Surviving Mumbai,” relays these events from the vantage points of a sprawling international ensemble. The characters, many of them composites, are the guests and staff members of the Taj Mahal Palace &amp; Tower Hotel, one of two luxury hotels the terrorists targeted, and where more than 30 died during the siege.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 An affluent couple (Nazanin Boniadi and Armie Hammer) leave their newborn upstairs with the nanny (Tilda Cobham-Hervey) to enjoy their dinner date in the hotel restaurant. A high-rolling Russian (Jason Isaacs) plans to spend the evening cavorting with local escorts. The heroic hotel employees include the head chef (Anupam Kher) and a Sikh waiter (Dev Patel) who shows up to work that day without proper footwear but begs to stay, needing the shift.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Anthony Maras, making his first feature, interweaves these threads with precision and clarity, conveying an impressive sense of the hotel layout, the confusion of the circumstances and the visceral fear of hiding from the gunmen. (The opulent hotel was re-created in both Mumbai and Adelaide, Australia.)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 But the more involving “Hotel Mumbai” plays in the moment, the queasier it seems in retrospect. It reduces the randomness of real-life bloodshed to the slick thrills of a popcorn movie. And after the mosque attacks in Christchurch, which
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nzherald.co.nz/entertainment/news/article.cfm?c_id=1501119&amp;objectid=12213821" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  led the film’s distributor in New Zealand to suspend the movie’s release there,
 </a>
 its savagery is especially difficult to take.
</p><p class="css-1bntlxc">
 When you purchase a ticket for an independently reviewed film through our site, we earn an affiliate commission.
</p><p>

</p><p>
 Rated R. Unceasing violence.
 <em>
 </em>
 In English, Urdu and Hindi, with English subtitles. Running time: 2 hours 3 minutes.
</p><p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">

</p><p class="css-z6dj7x e1wiw3jv0">
 Its superpower is letting black people just be themselves.
</p>
<p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
  Evan Narcisse
 </span>
</p>
<p class="css-ri4qrz e1wtpvyy1">
 Mr. Narcisse is the author of the “Rise of the Black Panther” graphic novel.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/02/06/movies/black-panther-review-movie.html?module=inline" title="">
  Black Panther
 </a>
 ,” which made
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.boxofficemojo.com/movies/?id=marvel2017b.htm" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  more than $1.3 billion last year
 </a>
 , became one of the highest-grossing superhero movies of all time. It did that by delivering a wide-screen vision of Black Excellence, where blackness isn’t just about the generational trauma and pain that comes from living under racism. It’s about reckoning with choices made to hold on to one’s culture and the best way to carry it into the future.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Watching it for the first time last year, I felt overjoyed. I tear up when I think of the scene just before T’Challa fights to become king, when Wakandans make their way to Warrior Falls in an elaborate floating procession, an expression of communal love for their history and culture. I have been immersed in the Black Panther universe for years as a comics fan, critic and creator. Yet I still felt, sitting there at the premiere in Los Angeles, that this was the kind of art that I had waited my entire life for.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Superhero fictions tend toward the aspirational, showing humanity in broadly idealized form, and that’s exactly what the director, Ryan Coogler, and his team did with “Black Panther.” Africa, in the film,
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2018/02/12/magazine/why-black-panther-is-a-defining-moment-for-black-america.html?module=inline" title="">
  is presented as a place where glorious achievement has always happened.
 </a>
 “Black Panther” feels like both a celebration and an investigation of what it means to be part of the global African diaspora.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 After buzzing off that high, I wondered what the next movie capable of speaking to me on that deep level might be. Would it be another sweeping Black Excellence melodrama? A slavery period piece with a fresh perspective? A sci-fi legal drama where a black criminal defense attorney has to represent extraterrestrial refugees? A thriller where assassins target a college basketball phenom organizing a movement to get him and his friends paid?
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The answer turned out to be something completely different:
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/2019/03/20/movies/us-movie-review.html?module=inline" title="">
  Jordan Peele’s new movie, “Us,”
 </a>
 which excites me for entirely different reasons. This stylish and utterly terrifying horror film doesn’t makes its black characters into superheroes, because it doesn’t have to. They are normal folks, albeit with one big creepy paranormal problem, and their complete everyday-ness is what makes this film so resonant.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “Us” isn’t about institutionalized racism. Its subject is the other tensions that run through the fabric of American life. The family at the center of the film are fighting for their lives, and their fight is a metaphor for the class tensions that periodically explode into ugly factionalism in the United States.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 In the film, metaphorical economic stratification turns some human beings into homicidal Others — they’re called Tethers in “Us.” And in a rare turn, the
 <!-- -->
 black people
 <!-- -->
 in the film, the Wilson family, aren’t the other. Adelaide, her husband, Gabe, and their two children occupy the position of the ordinary family who have their lives upended by the unsettling threat. They don’t respond to it with martial prowess or stoic durability, either. They freak the hell out, like any normal people would. Like the Griswolds in the “National Lampoon” movies, the Wilsons are just a family on vacation. The parents grumble and embrace, and the kids bicker, just like my siblings and I did when we went to Disneyworld.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 When the Wilsons inevitably come face to face with their evil doppelgängers, “Us” becomes a film about, among other things, unaddressed histories creeping back into your life. If Mr. Peele’s new film can be said to have a moral, it might be about the cost we pay, as individuals and as a society, for wanting to live the good life, which means burying past trauma and refusing to reckon with all of ourselves.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 That the director chooses to rest the prickly heart of his cinematic ambitions in a dark-skinned black woman, Lupita Nyong’o, makes it even more meaningful. Her Adelaide Wilson isn’t a resolute military leader, like Danai Gurira’s General Okoye in “Black Panther.” She’s a nurturing, nervous and neurotic mother, drawn with a nuance and texture that allows viewers to relate to her no matter what walk of life they might come from.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 My euphoria over “Black Panther” last year felt like one answer to a deep yearning to feel connected to African-descended people all over the world and see our shared roots reflected in glory. “Us” giddily fulfills a different, opposite need: a desire to see black life reflected back at life-size scale, not idealized or put on a pedestal. Hey, look, there’s Winston Duke busting out dad jokes, awkwardly having to explain his favorite old songs to his kids and putting some bass in his voice to seem more threatening than he actually really is. I do those things, and so do a lot of my friends.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 I’ve spent a lot of time thinking about what comes after Wakanda, and “Us” points the way to the broader horizons Hollywood should explore. Mr. Peele’s new film presents its black characters the same way it does its white ones. The specific texture of their blackness is still palpable, in the way they talk and react to the world around them, but it doesn’t have to have any special meaning. It’s just part of who they are, and they could be any of the millions of other black people all over the world. In “Us,” the black performers don’t need to be symbols. They can just be, even with boogeypeople chasing them onscreen. For all the horror and mystery in “Us,” this element of charming ordinariness gives me a little bit of hope for the movies coming in a post-“Black Panther” world.
</p><p class="css-1psfkbx etfikam0">
 Evan Narcisse (
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://twitter.com/evnarc?lang=en" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  @EvNarc
 </a>
 ) is a senior staff writer at io9 and the author of the “Rise of the Black Panther” graphic novel.
</p>

</p><p class="css-1jc06xn e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
  Judge John Hodgman
 </span>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <strong class="css-8qgvsz ebyp5n10">
  Jayson writes:
 </strong>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  In my family, we watch a new movie every Friday night. However, my mother always brings out her phone halfway through and reads the ending on Wikipedia. This is not fair for two reasons: I do not have a phone, and it is an insult to the filmmakers and actors.
 </em>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 My guess is your mom is just trying to understand what is happening in the movie. Your mom and I are not as quick as we used to be, and it is easy to lose the plot as our minds wander to thoughts of the past and death and yearned-for naps. But you’re right that she’s not honoring the spirit of movie night, which is togetherness. Turn the subtitles on — they help! — and put the phone down, Mom! Meanwhile, I can’t order her to give you a phone (nice try), but when you do get one, I encourage you to go into Wikipedia and change the ending of any movie you’re watching, in case she lapses.
</p><p>

 Advertisement
</p><p>
 Supported by
</p><p class="css-c2jxua e6idgb70">
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="/column/grace-notes" title="Grace Notes">
  Grace Notes
 </a>
</p>
<p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <a class="css-1riqqik e1jsehar0" href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/james-barron">
  <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
   James Barron
  </span>
 </a>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  [What you need to know to start the day:
 </em>
 <a href="https://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/newyorktoday?module=inline">
  <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.nytimes.com/newsletters/newyorktoday?module=inline" title="">
   Get New York Today in your inbox
  </a>
 </a>
 <em class="css-2fg4z9 e1gzwzxm0">
  .]
 </em>
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 For the 9:20 p.m. movie, Jesse Locascio threaded film through the projector. This was after he had sprayed compressed air on the sprockets and rollers, blowing out dust that could show up as black dots on the screen.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “This is not how they do it at the multiplex,” he said. “Not even close.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Not in the digital age.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Mr. Locascio, 28, is a movie projectionist who can do things the old-fashioned way, operating projectors with big reels of celluloid. Projectionists, he says, are a dying breed and he learned much of what he knows in a long, narrow room of clattering machinery: a projectionist’s booth at the
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://burnsfilmcenter.org/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Jacob Burns Film Center
 </a>
 in Pleasantville, N.Y.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The Jacob Burns, as it is known, operates a media arts lab that teaches digital literacy as well as a nonprofit art house that was screening
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.imdb.com/title/tt0066563/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  “Where’s Poppa?”
 </a>
 — a 1970 comedy directed by Carl Reiner — at 9:20 in theater two. But it also trains projectionists, and in an art house, that means learning to handle more than digital files.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 It means learning to handle celluloid.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “It was a whole new education,” said Jesse Modica, who as the center’s technical director is Mr. Locascio’s boss. Mr. Modica had worked in commercial theaters before he arrived at the Jacob Burns in 2007.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “My previous training didn’t include any of the nuances,” he said, but he learned from the projectionists at the Jacob Burns. And now Mr. Modica trains newcomers like Mr. Locascio in the mechanics of showing film.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Training a projectionist is “like giving somebody private lessons,” Mr. Modica said. “A lot of this is muscle memory. Once I teach someone, ‘O.K., this is how you inspect the film, this is how you get the sound to work, this is how you make sure you’ve got the right aspect ratio,’ they’ve got to remember come showtime. It’s like someone taking karate. You can learn all the karate you want in the dojo, but are you going to remember when you’re being attacked in an alley?”
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The Jacob Burns has five theaters. It can show digital films in all of them and celluloid in three. Even then the newest of the projectors are almost antiques. Mr. Modica said they are only 10 years old, but the company that made them has gone out of business.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The projectors running “Where’s Poppa?” are older: They were originally used at Lincoln Center, Mr. Modica said, and probably date to the 1970s.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 For generations, movie theaters had two projectors for each screen. The movies were cut into segments, each about 20 minutes long and each on a separate reel. Every 20 minutes, as the end of one reel approached, the projectionist had to start the next reel on the other projector.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 There was a reason for the two-projector arrangement: the bulb in the projector had a life span of not much longer than 20 minutes. The projectionist had to install a new bulb every time he — and most projectionists were men in those days — changed a reel. Longer-lasting bulbs were perfected in the 1960s and 1970s, and with them reel-like platters big enough for a whole movie to fit on just one.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 That changed the labor equation. Projectionists became what Mr. Modica calls “a niche trade.” In the 1940s, there were more than 30,000 projectionists nationwide and 2,400 in New York City, but with the rise of television in the 1950s, “the number declined as more Americans chose to stay home and watch TV,” the labor economists Robert D. Atkinson and John Wu wrote in 2017 in a
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://itif.org/publications/2017/05/08/false-alarmism-technological-disruption-and-us-labor-market-1850-2015" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  paper published
 </a>
 by the
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://itif.org/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  Information Technology and Innovation Foundation
 </a>
 .
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 By 2015, there were fewer than 5,000 nationally. “There’s a supply shortage,” said Douglas McLaren, the manager of Cornell Cinema, the campus film exhibition program at Cornell University. “There are not enough jobs. Also, film exhibition now is becoming a rarefied thing, so the old-school projection mentality, when the profession employed thousands of people and film prints were expendable, is gone. There’s been a need to step up the quality of work that projectionists do.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 That has happened as film has all but disappeared from most theaters. Most large chain theaters “have zero film,” said Tal Marks, a former chief projectionist at the Museum of Modern Art who is now the museum’s audiovisual technical manager. “The mainstream moviegoing audience is not seeing film anymore. The blockbuster hits are digital representations.”
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 And in most multiplexes, the theater managers run the projectors, which is how Mr. Modica got his start, at a chain-owned theater in Bronxville, N.Y.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 He began as a 16-year-old usher in 1995, the year “Toy Story,” “Die Hard With a Vengeance” and “Apollo 13” came out.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The first time he went into the projection booth and saw the film going into the projector, “I was in awe, I was scared, I didn’t want to touch it, I didn’t want to be responsible for anything going wrong.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The theater manager doubled as the projectionist. Mr. Modica learned the basics of projection after he was promoted to assistant manager at another theater in the chain a couple of years later.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Mr. Locascio also started as an usher when he was a teenager and went on to study filmmaking at the School of Visual Arts in Manhattan. After he graduated in 2012, the Jacob Burns hired him as a projectionist, offering on-the-job training, even though he had learned about film editing at S.V.A.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “I do feel it’s a dying field, the film end of things,” Mr. Locascio said. Still, he added, “with digital, you still need someone there for quality control, making sure the volume is right, the brightness is right.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 When he is not behind the projectors at the Jacob Burns, he works on film crews as a focus puller, responsible for keeping the camera focused when the director yells “Action.” He has worked on the Netflix movie “White Girl,” the documentary “The Hunting Ground,” and the teen horror film “The Ranger.” The Jacob Burns schedules him as a projectionist three or four times a week.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Movies like “Where’s Poppa?” are sent by a film archive and are treated with care. “They’re not being mass produced anymore,” Mr. Modica said. “We can’t say, ‘Hey distributor, give me another reel,’ because there isn’t another reel.” When a movie arrives from an archive, “We have to sign a release saying we will return this print in the condition it arrived in and if anything is wrong we accept responsibility. That puts a lot of pressure on.”
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Is digital less stressful?
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Yes and no, Mr. Modica said. “In the celluloid world,” he said, “a good projectionist could fix any technical or mechanical malfunction on the fly within minutes.” Digital projection is moving a projectionist’s duties “toward an IT job instead of a mechanical job.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “The way these things work now, if there’s corrupt software, a projectionist can’t fix that in a few minutes,” he said. There are calls to a service desk somewhere. “If rebooting your server to your projector doesn’t fix whatever problem you’re having, you’re down” until a technician can come.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 At 9:20, Mr. Locascio flipped a toggle switch marked “projector motor.” The clicking started. On the screen, the credits rolled.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 In the booth, there was no time to follow the plot or laugh at the sight gags. Mr. Modica moved a digital projector out of the way so that he could reach the other reel-to-reel projector, and loaded the second reel of “Where’s Poppa?” On the screen, George Segal was taking a shower and shaving.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 A few minutes later, a man in a gorilla suit was terrorizing Ruth Gordon, but Mr. Locascio did not notice. He was concerned about something else.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “When is the next show?” he asked.
</p><p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>
<p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>
<p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>
<p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>
<p>
 Advertisement
</p><p>
 Advertisement
</p><p>
 Supported by
</p><p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
  Rachel Saltz
 </span>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “Got porn, Madam?” That’s what the high school boy known variously as Milk Carton and Egg Muffin asks a DVD store clerk after some stammering feints in the Tamil movie “Super Deluxe.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 And, yes, Madam has porn. But, surprise: When Egg Muffin and his pals start to watch it, one of them becomes enraged. That’s his mother onscreen.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 This sets off a chain of mostly comic events that are, by turns, ominous, bloody and cosmic. And that’s just one plot strand. In another, a married woman’s ex-boyfriend dies in her bed, setting off a chain of comic, ominous events. In a third, a little boy pines for his father to return, and the father does — but now transformed into a woman. (Another chain ensues.)
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The director Thiagarajan Kumararaja, who also had a hand in the script, takes his time setting all these shaggy, laconic story lines in motion. Part of the movie’s pleasure in its early going is figuring out whether and how they will all merge.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Another pleasure is visual. Colors pop off Kumararaja’s palette (the cinematographers are P.S. Vinod and Nirav Shah), and there’s always something to look at in his Chennai. This isn’t gleaming, ascendant India; it’s the lived in one, crumbling around the edges, a little romanticized but recognizable in its narrow alleys and concrete stairwells and power outages.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “Super Deluxe,” Kumararaja’s second feature, has been a while in coming after “Aaranya Kaandam” (2010), which was a critic’s darling. No wonder — Kumararaja’s work is stylish and wry, with an indie-cinephile sensibility. (It’s no accident that “Kill Bill” and
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.rogerebert.com/reviews/gangs-of-wasseypur-2015" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  “Gangs of Wasseypur”
 </a>
 posters hang on the DVD store’s wall.)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Part of that sensibility is a frankness about sex that’s still unusual in Indian movies, especially commercial ones. Though nothing explicit is shown, all the story lines in “Super Deluxe” have a little sexual motor, and there’s plenty of frank, off-color language, too.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Kumararaja also elicits some wonderfully deadpan performances from his actors. The teenage boys have a believably nerdy-raffish rapport And Samantha Akkineni, as the cheating wife, builds a character of unexpected depths.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “Super Deluxe,” though, runs three hours, and Kumararaja loses his way in the draggy, overlong second act. It includes not one, but two drawn-out scenes of threatened rape. (We know the ugly outcome of one, though it happens off camera.)
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 That these scenes, with their leering Bollywood-ish villain, verge on the cartoonish doesn’t save them. They’re part of a tonal problem — what was mostly delicate and offbeat tips into something cruder and messier. They also serve as a reminder: Cinematic sexual liberation for (and by) men can be punishing for women.
</p><p>
 Movie data powered by IMDb.com
</p><p>
 Super Deluxe
 <br/>
 Not rated. In Tamil, with English subtitles. Running time: 2 hours 56 minutes.
</p><p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>
<p class="css-zdpruj e6idgb70">
</p>

</p><p>
 Supported by
</p><p class="css-4oyyp6">
 When you purchase a ticket for an independently reviewed film through our site, we earn an affiliate commission.
</p><p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <a class="css-1riqqik e1jsehar0" href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/ben-kenigsberg">
  <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
   Ben Kenigsberg
  </span>
 </a>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 It’s clear why filmmakers can’t resist the allure of Alfred Hitchcock even if they inevitably fall short of him. But his imitators rarely come up with movies as pointless as “Naples in Veils,” which tips its hat vigorously toward the master’s motifs — the lost-and-found doubling of “Vertigo,” the repressed trauma of “Marnie,” possibly even Salvador Dalí’s eye design from “Spellbound” — only to wind up as a tedious muddle.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Adriana (Giovanna Mezzogiorno), a pathologist in Naples who is shy with men, has a one-night stand with Andrea (Alessandro Borghi), a much younger diver. The sex is rendered with a level of detail that seems more suited to a Euro-art remake of “Fifty Shades of Grey” than a Hitchcock riff — but then again, it’s supposed to become an obsession for Adriana. They make plans to meet at a museum the next day, except Andrea doesn’t show up.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Or rather, he has shown up dead in a nearby dumpster, something Adriana learns soon after starting an autopsy on his corpse. But then she spots him, alive, in a subway station. And in her garden. And did we mention that her mother apparently went crazy?
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Promising ideas are in place; even using as a backdrop a city as rich in history and steeped in Catholicism as Naples might have appealed to Hitch. But the director, Ferzan Ozpetek, hasn’t found a way to bring his borrowed themes together with style, momentum or suspense. Visually and musically, “Naples in Veils” does little to conjure a sensation of passion or delirium. All that’s left, in this case, is a self-nullifying plot.
</p><p class="css-1bntlxc">

 ROME, Ga. — A Georgia man who has acted in zombie movies is accused of beating two women and forcing one of them to taste his blood.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The Rome News-Tribune reports 30-year-old Eliot Ryan Rutledge faces charges including false imprisonment and aggravated assault.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Rutledge allegedly trapped a woman inside his home in Rome, Georgia, in August 2018 and assaulted her by punching, elbowing and poking her. Floyd County Jail documents report the same woman was assaulted at his home between June 2017 and October 2018.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 He's also accused of hurting another woman in January by choking and biting her and forcing his cut hand into her mouth to make her taste his blood.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 IMDB.com credits Rutledge with producing and acting in the 2017 short films "Gangsters and Zombies" and "Gangsters and Zombies II."

</p><p class="css-4oyyp6">
 When you purchase a ticket for an independently reviewed film through our site, we earn an affiliate commission.
</p><p class="css-16vrk19 e1jsehar1" itemid="" itemprop="author creator" itemscope="" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
 By
 <!-- -->
 <a class="css-1riqqik e1jsehar0" href="https://www.nytimes.com/by/glenn-kenny">
  <span class="css-1baulvz" itemprop="name">
   Glenn Kenny
  </span>
 </a>
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 Setting a stage musical inside a stalled subway car sounds like such a natural idea that I’m surprised there hasn’t been a whole bunch of such shows. But after watching the film adaptation of
 <a class="css-1g7m0tk" href="https://www.backstage.com/magazine/article/ny-review-stuck-52074/" rel="noopener noreferrer" target="_blank" title="">
  the 2012 musical
 </a>
 “Stuck,” directed by Michael Berry, I’m sorry anybody ever thought of the concept at all.
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 “Everyone has a story like yours within them,” sings the sagacious homeless man Lloyd (Giancarlo Esposito, who, like most of the cast, somehow manages to get through this with his dignity intact). The stories of the five other passengers on the stationary subway car aim to show how, yes, we’re all connected and yes, we all need one another.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 The pregnant woman (Ashanti) who intends to terminate her pregnancy needs to hear from the grieving mother (Amy Madigan) who lost her adult son to cancer. The sullen dancer (Arden Cho) needs to learn that the floppy-haired guy who seems to have been stalking her, to the point of following her onto this very car (Gerard Canonico), is not a stalker as such, but a sensitive artist.
</p><p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 And so on. Underneath the ostensible humanism of the scenarios, fleshed out in songs by the playwright Riley Thomas with Tim Young and Ben Maughan, seems to lurk a mind-set more attuned to “Father Knows Best” than “Rent.”
</p>
<p class="css-1ygdjhk evys1bk0">
 When the characters are singing, you can’t wait for them to get back to talking. And when they’re talking, you can’t wait for them to get back to singing. After a while, you start wishing you were watching that TV ad with a bunch of people on a bus, singing about how they have a structured settlement but they need cash now. That spot gets its point across in less time, and has better music.
</p><p class="css-1bntlxc">
 When you purchase a ticket for an independently reviewed film through our site, we earn an affiliate commission.
</p><p>

</p><p>
 Rated PG-13 for language and themes and stalkers-not-stalkers. Running time: 1 hour 30 minutes.
